Tasawwur and Tasdiq
beginner
7 min
Tasawwur and Tasdiq: The Two Modes of Knowledge
Defines tasawwur/tasdiq and resolves the unity problem

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tasawwur and tasdiq, state the objection of their unity, and explain how the author resolves it using the dual aspect of the mental image.

Core Idea
Conception and assent share one mental image; judgment alone differentiates them.

Before you can wield logic like a master, you must understand the two fundamental ways we know anything. This lesson unpacks the very first move in Islamic epistemology: the distinction between simple conception (tasawwur) and judgmental assent (tasdiq). A famous objection threatens to collapse the distinction, but the author offers a subtle resolution that preserves both unity and difference.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of knowledge
Read the first few lines where the author defines knowledge as 'that which is present to the perceiver'. Notice that it is said to be self-evident yet hard to refine. This sets the stage for the two types.
The term 'ilm here is broad, covering both conception and assent.
2
Distinguish tasawwur from tasdiq
The author states: if the mental content involves belief in a predicative relation (a proposition), it is tasdiq; otherwise it is tasawwur. Mark the key difference: judgment. They are two distinct types of perception.
Tasawwur is like having a picture; tasdiq is like affirming a caption.
3
Understand the unity objection
The objection says: knowledge and the known are identical in essence. So if you conceive of assent, the conception and the known (assent) would be the same, contradicting the claim that they are different. Identify the apparent paradox.
This is a serious challenge – if true, the whole foundation of logic collapses.
4
Follow the resolution via the mental image
The author resolves by distinguishing two aspects of the same mental image: as existing in the mind it is 'known', as subsisting in the soul it is 'knowledge'. The image becomes knowledge when a perceptual state mixes with it, like taste with a tastable object. Then the same underlying state can be either conception or assent depending on presence of judgment.
The analogy with sleep and wakefulness helps: the same person can be both, but they are distinct states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن سلم العلوم
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
سبحانه، ما أعظم شانه، لا يحد، ولا يتصور، لا ينتج، ولا يتغير، تعالى عن الجنس والجهات، جعل الكليات والجزئيات الإيمان به نعم التصديق، والاعتصام به حبذا التوفيق، والصلاة والسلام على من بعث بالدليل الذي فيه شفاء لكل عليل، وعلى آله وأصحابه الذين هم مقدمات الدين، حجج الهداية واليقين، أما بعد، فهذه رسالة في صناعة الميزان، سميتها بـ'سلم العلوم'، اللهم اجعلها بين المتون كالشمس بين النجوم.
مقدمة: العلم التصور، وهو الحاضر عند المدرك، والحق أنه من أجل البديهيات، كالنور والسرور، نعم، تنقيح حقيقته عسير جدا، فإن كان اعتقادا لنسبة خبرية فتصديق وحكم، وإلا فتصور ساذج، وهما نوعان متباينان من الإدراك ضرورة، نعم، لا حجر في التصور، فيتعلق بكل شيء.
وههنا شك مشهور، وهو: أن العلم والمعلوم متحدان بالذات، فإذا تصورنا التصديق فهما واحد، وقد قلتم إنهما متخالفان حقيقة.
وحله ـ على ما تفرّدت به - أن العلم في مسألة الانحاد بمعنى الصورة العلمية؛ فإنها من حيث الحصول في الذهن معلوم، ومن حيث القيام به علم، ثم بعد التفتيش يعلم أن تلك الصورة إنما صارت علما؛ لأن الحالة الإدراكية قد خالطت بوجودها الانطباعي، خلطا رابطيا اتحاديا، كالحالة الذوقية بالمذوقات، فصارت صورة ذوقية، والسمعية بالمسموعات، وهكذا، فتلك الحالة تنقسم إلى التصور والتصديق، فتفاوتهما كتفاوت النوم واليقظة العارضتين لذات واحدة، المتباينتين بحسب حقيقتهما، فتفكر.
Text of Sullam al-'Ulum. In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Glory be to Him, how great is His status; He cannot be defined, nor imagined; He does not produce (conclusions) nor changes; Exalted above genus and directions; He made the universals and particulars; faith in Him is the best assent, and clinging to Him is an excellent success. And blessings and peace be upon the one sent with the proof in which is healing for every sick one, and upon his family and companions who are the introductions of the religion, the proofs of guidance and certainty. To proceed: This is a treatise on the craft of the balance; I have named it 'Sullam al-'Ulum'. O Allah, make it among the texts like the sun among the stars. Introduction: Knowledge is conception, and it is what is present to the perceiver; the truth is that it is among the most evident of self-evident things, like light and pleasure. Yes, refining its reality is very difficult. If it is a belief in a predicative relation, then it is assent and judgment; otherwise it is a simple conception. They are two mutually distinct types of perception by necessity. Yes, there is no restriction in conception, so it relates to everything. And here is a famous doubt: that knowledge and the known are identical in essence; so if we conceive of assent, they are one, while you have said they are different in reality. Its resolution—according to what I uniquely hold—is that 'knowledge' in the issue of unification is in the sense of the mental image; for it, from the aspect of its occurrence in the mind, is known, and from the aspect of its subsisting (with the soul) is knowledge. Then after investigation it is known that that image only becomes knowledge because the perceptual state has mixed with its imprinting existence, a relational unitive mixture, like the taste state with tasted objects, so it becomes a taste-image, and the auditory with sounds, and so on. Thus that state divides into conception and assent; their difference is like the difference between sleep and wakefulness that accidentally affect a single essence, which are mutually distinct according to their reality. So reflect.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage from the matn 'Sullam al-'Ulum' and its sharh (commentary) introduces the foundational distinction between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). The author begins with praise and then states that this treatise is on the 'craft of the balance' (logic). In the introduction, 'knowledge' is defined as 'that which is present to the perceiver', i.e., the form of an object in the mind. It is said to be self-evident but hard to refine. Then the two types of knowledge are defined: if the mental content involves belief in a predicative relation (a proposition), it is assent; otherwise it is simple conception. The two are necessarily distinct. Then a famous objection is raised: knowledge and the known are identical in essence, so if we have a conception of assent, knowledge and known would be the same, contradicting their claimed difference. The unique solution given is that the term 'knowledge' in this objection refers to the mental image itself. This image, from one aspect (its occurrence in the mind) is the known, and from another aspect (its subsisting in the soul) is knowledge. Through investigation, we realize that the image becomes knowledge because a perceptual state mixes with it in a unitive manner, analogous to taste when tasting. Thus the same underlying state can be both conception and assent in different respects, just as the same person can be both asleep and awake at different times. The distinction remains real, not contradictory.

Key Terms
العلم
التصور
التصديق
البديهي
النظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, how is the problem of the unity of knowledge and the known resolved?
A) By distinguishing between the mental image as known and its subsistence as knowledge.
B) By denying that knowledge and known are ever identical.
C) By asserting that all knowledge is judgmental.
D) By claiming that tasawwur and tasdiq are the same thing.

Key Takeaway
Tasawwur and tasdiq are distinct modes of knowledge that share the same mental image; their difference lies in the presence or absence of judgment, and this is not contradicted by the unity of knowledge and the known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'The world is temporally originated.' Is that tasawwur or tasdiq for a person who understands the words but does not believe them? Explain using the author's definition.
?
A mutakallim argues: 'I have a conception of the Creator's attributes, so I have faith.' Using this lesson, explain why conception alone is not sufficient for faith; assent is also needed.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Why Some Knowledge Must Be Self-Evident
Author argues that knowledge must include both self-evident and acquired types to avoid circularity/infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that if all knowledge were acquired through reflection, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress, and thus some knowledge must be self-evident.

Core Idea
To avoid circularity and infinite regress, some knowledge must be self-evident.

In this lesson, we explore a foundational argument in Islamic epistemology: why not all knowledge can be acquired through reasoning. The author demonstrates that if every piece of knowledge depended on prior reflection, we would be caught in an endless chain or a circle, making knowledge impossible. Therefore, some knowledge must be self-evident (badihi). This argument is crucial for understanding the structure of knowledge in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
The author considers two extremes: either all knowledge is self-evident (badihi) or all is acquired (nazari). He shows both cannot be the case universally.
2
Identify the consequences of the 'all nazari' assumption
If all knowledge required prior reasoning, then each piece of knowledge depends on another, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul).
Circularity means A depends on B and B depends on A; infinite regress means an endless chain of dependencies.
3
Analyze the absurdity of infinite regress
Infinite regress is false because it would require increasing an infinite number, which is impossible; the author gives a detailed argument about numbers.
The argument uses the idea that the number of detailed steps is greater than the original, and if the original were infinite, an infinite would be exceeded, which is absurd.
4
Conclude that some knowledge must be self-evident
Since circularity and infinite regress are impossible, not all knowledge can be acquired; some must be self-evident.
This conclusion applies to both tasdiq (assent) and tasawwur (conception): part of each is badihi.
5
Apply the same reasoning to conception and assent
The author extends the argument: just as with propositions, some conceptions and assents are self-evident and some are acquired. Simple terms (basit) cannot be acquired, so they must be known directly.
This shows that the need for badihi knowledge is fundamental to all types of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس الكل من كل منهما بديهيا، وإلا فأنت مستغن، ولا نظريا متوقفا على النظر، وإلا لدار، فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه بمرتبتين، بل بمراتب غير متناهية، فإن الدور مستلزم للتسلسل، أو تسلسل، وهو باطل؛ لأن عدد التفصيل أزيد من عدد الأصل، وكل عددين أحدهما أزيد من الآخر فزيادة الزائد بعد انصرام جميع آحاد المزيد عليه؛ فإن المبدأ لا يتصور عليه الزيادة، والأوساط منتظمة متوالية، فحينئذ لو كان المزيد عليه غير متناه لزم الزيادة في جانب عدم التناهي، وهو باطل، وتناهي العدد يستلزم تناهي المعدود، فتدبر. ولا يعلم التصور من التصديق وبالعكس، لأن المعرّف مقول، والتصور متساوي النسبة، فبعض كل واحد منهما بديهي، وبعضه نظري، والبسيط لا يكون كاسبا، فلا بد من ترتيب أمور للاكتساب، وهو النظر والفكر.
وههنا شك خوطب به سقراط، وهو أن المطلوب إما معلوم، فالطلب تحصيل الحاصل، وإما مجهول، فكيف الطلب؟ وأجيب بأنه معلوم من وجه ومجهول من وجه، فعاد قائلاً: الوجه المعلوم معلوم والوجه المجهول مجهول.
And not everything from each of them is self-evident; otherwise you would be independent. Nor is everything acquired, depending on reflection; otherwise there would be circularity, leading to the thing preceding itself by two ranks, or even by infinite ranks. For circularity necessitates infinite regress, or it is an infinite regress, and that is false. Because the number of the detailed (parts) is greater than the number of the original; and any two numbers where one is greater than the other, the increase of the greater occurs after the cessation of all the units of the lesser; for the beginning is not conceived to be increased upon, and the intermediates are arranged successively. Then if the lesser were infinite, an increase would be required on the side of infinity, which is false. And the finitude of number necessitates the finitude of the numbered. So ponder. And conception is not known from assent nor vice versa, because the definer is predicated, and conception is of equal relation (to both). So part of each of them is self-evident, and part is acquired. The simple (term) cannot be acquiring, so there must be an arrangement of things for acquisition, which is reflection and thought. And here is a doubt with which Socrates was addressed: that the sought is either known, so the seeking is the acquisition of the already acquired, or it is unknown, so how can one seek it? It was answered: it is known in one aspect and unknown in another. But he replied: the known aspect is known and the unknown aspect is unknown.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage, continuing the sharh, argues that not all knowledge can be self-evident (badihi) nor can it all be acquired (nazari). If all were badihi, we would be independent of reasoning, which is false. If all were nazari, then knowledge of each thing would depend on prior knowledge, leading to circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The author explains that circularity necessarily leads to infinite regress, and infinite regress is absurd because it would require an increase in an infinite quantity, which is impossible. Therefore, some knowledge must be badihi and some nazari. Moreover, conception is not defined by assent nor vice versa, because they are distinct. Some of each are badihi and some nazari. Simple concepts cannot be acquired; we must arrange things to acquire knowledge through reflection (nazar) and thought (fikr). The passage then presents the famous Socratic puzzle: How can we seek something if it is already known (pointless) or totally unknown (impossible)? The answer given is that the sought is known in one respect (e.g., its genus) and unknown in another (e.g., its differentia). Socrates's counter-argument is that the known respect is known and the unknown respect is unknown, so the problem remains. The text does not provide a further resolution but presents the dialectic.

Key Terms
الدور
التسلسل
النظر
البسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's argument against the possibility that all knowledge is acquired (nazari)?
A) It would lead to an infinite regress of dependencies.
B) It would make all knowledge self-evident.
C) It would contradict the Socratic puzzle.
D) It would make definition impossible.

Key Takeaway
To avoid circularity and infinite regress, knowledge must include both self-evident and acquired types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a premise in a standard Kalam argument that is considered self-evident (badihi). Explain why it must be badihi to avoid circularity.
?
Write a brief argument showing that if all knowledge were nazari, it would lead to an infinite regress, using a fiqh example: e.g., each ruling depends on a prior ruling.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

The Socratic Puzzle: Seeking the Unknown
Author presents the Socratic puzzle and a response, with a counter-objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Socratic puzzle about inquiry and the response that the sought is known in one aspect and unknown in another.

Core Idea
Inquiry is possible because we partially know and partially do not know the object.

This lesson examines a famous epistemological puzzle attributed to Socrates: How can we seek something we do not know? The puzzle challenges the very possibility of learning. The response that the sought is known in one respect and unknown in another is a key insight in Islamic logic, often used to explain how ijtihad and intellectual investigation proceed.

Guided Path
1
State the puzzle
The puzzle presents a dilemma: the object of inquiry is either already known or completely unknown. If known, seeking is pointless; if unknown, how can one even begin to seek it?
2
Identify the dilemma
The puzzle highlights a paradox: inquiry seems impossible in either case. This challenges the very act of learning.
3
Present the response
The response is that the sought is known in one aspect (e.g., its genus or general characteristics) and unknown in another (e.g., its specific differentia). Thus, inquiry is possible by moving from the known to the unknown.
4
Consider the counter-objection
Socrates objects: the known aspect is known and the unknown aspect is unknown, so the dilemma remains. The text leaves this unresolved, but it prompts deeper reflection.
The counter-objection forces a refinement: the known aspect must be relevant to the inquiry, not trivial.
5
Reflect on the implication
The puzzle shows that inquiry requires a partial knowledge to proceed; thus, the distinction between badihi and nazari knowledge remains useful. In Islamic logic, this is often resolved by noting that the seeker has a mental representation (tasawwur) of the sought, even if incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the response to the Socratic puzzle?
A) The sought is entirely unknown.

Key Takeaway
The Socratic puzzle is resolved by recognizing that the object of inquiry is known in some respects (making inquiry possible) and unknown in other respects (making it necessary).

?

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate

Why Tasawwur Precedes Tasdiq
Author explains priority of tasawwur and resolves objection about judging the unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why tasawwur is naturally prior to tasdiq and resolve the objection about judging the unknown using the distinction between essential and accidental knowledge.

Core Idea
You cannot judge what you do not conceive.

This passage opens the discussion of conceptualizations (tasawwurat) by justifying why they are presented before assents (tasdiq). The author argues that the absolutely unknown cannot be judged, but resolves a potential objection by distinguishing between being known in essence and known accidentally. This distinction is crucial for understanding how we form valid judgments about unseen realities in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states that tasawwurat are presented first because they are naturally prior. Understand that for judgment to occur, the subject must be known in some way.
The priority is natural (tab'an), not just conventional.
2
Understand the objection
An objector says: if you judge the absolutely unknown, that judgment would be false. This highlights the problem of judging without prior conception.
The objection assumes complete ignorance.
3
Examine the solution
The author resolves this by distinguishing between knowing something in essence (bi-dhat) and knowing it accidentally (bi-'l-'arad). The absolutely unknown is known in essence but unknown accidentally, allowing judgment and its negation to apply based on these two considerations.
This distinction saves the possibility of judgment.
4
Apply to a theological example
In proving the existence of Allah, one must first have a concept (tasawwur) of 'existence' and 'Allah' before affirming the judgment that Allah exists, even if the essence of Allah is not fully known.
The concept of God is known in essence (through general attributes) but unknown in His precise reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التَّصَوُّرات
قَدَّمْنَاهَا وَضْعاً لِتَقَدُّمِهَا طَبَعًا ، فَإِنَّ الْمَجْهُولَ الْمُطْلَقَ يَمْتَنِعُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ قِيلَ: فِيهِ حُكْمٌ فَهُوَ كَذِبٌ ، وَحَلُهُ : أَنَّهُ مَعْلُومٌ بِالذَّاتِ ، وَمَجْهُولٌ مُطلَق بِالْعَرَضِ، فَالْحُكْمُ وَسَلْبُهَ بِالاعْتِبَارَيْنِ ، وَسَيَأْتِي.
الْإِفَادَةُ إِنَّمَا تَتِمُّ بِالدَّلَالَةِ، مِنْهَا عَقْلِيَّةٌ بِعَلَاقَةٍ ذَاتِيَّةٍ، وَمِنْهَا: وَضْعِيَّةٌ، بِجَعْل الْجَاعِلِ ، وَمِنْهَا : طَبَعِيَّةٌ ، بإحْدَاثِ الطَّبِيعَةِ ، وَكُلِّ مِنْهَا لَفْظِيَّةٌ وَغَيْرُ لَفْظِيَّةٌ.
Conceptualizations (tasawwurat): We have presented them in order of position (wad'an) due to their priority by nature (tab'an). For it is impossible to judge the absolutely unknown. It was said: In that case there would be judgment, so it would be false. Its solution: It is known in essence, and absolutely unknown accidentally. So judgment and its negation are by the two considerations. This will come later.
Communication (ifada) is only completed by indication (dalala). Some of it is rational ('aqliyya) by a inherent relation; some of it is conventional (wad'iyya) by the making of a maker; some of it is natural (tabi'iyya) by the effect of nature. And each of them is verbal (lafziyya) or non-verbal (ghayr lafziyya).
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on tasawwurat. It first explains why tasawwur is presented first: because it is naturally prior to tasdiq; one cannot judge something completely unknown. A potential objection—that such a judgment would be false—is resolved by distinguishing between being known in essence and unknown accidentally. Judgment and its negation apply accordingly (the full explanation is deferred). Then the passage introduces the concept of indication (dalala) as necessary for communication. Indication is divided into three types: rational ('aqliyya), which relies on a inherent relationship (like smoke indicating fire); conventional (wad'iyya), which relies on a maker's assignment (like words indicating meanings); and natural (tabi'iyya), which relies on nature's effect (like a pale face indicating fear). Each type can be verbal or non-verbal. This classification is foundational for understanding how words convey meanings in logic and linguistics.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)
حكم (hukm)
مجهول مطلق (majhul mutlaq)
ذاتي (dhati)
عرضي ('aradi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The unknown is known accidentally.
B) The unknown is known in essence but unknown accidentally.
C) The unknown is impossible to judge.
D) The objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The priority of concept over assent is grounded in the impossibility of judging the unknown; the solution lies in the distinction between essential and accidental knowledge.

The Three Types of Indication (Dalala)
Author classifies indication into three types and notes they can be verbal or non-verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate the three types of indication (rational, conventional, natural) and classify any given indicator as verbal or non-verbal.

Core Idea
Indication is the bridge to communication, and it comes from reason, convention, or nature.

Having established the importance of concepts, the author now explains how meanings are communicated through indication. This passage classifies indication into three fundamental types based on the source of the relationship between the indicator and the indicated. Understanding this classification is essential for interpreting texts, signs, and natural phenomena in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the role of indication
The passage states that communication (ifada) is only completed through indication (dalala). Indication is the means by which something conveys meaning.
Without indication, no meaning can be transmitted.
2
Learn the three types
Indication is divided into three: rational ('aqliyya) based on an inherent relation; conventional (wad'iyya) based on a maker's assignment; natural (tabi'iyya) based on natural causation.
Each type has a distinct basis: inherent essence, human convention, or nature.
3
Note the verbal/non-verbal distinction
Each type of indication can be either verbal (lafziyya) or non-verbal (ghayr lafziyya). For example, the sound of thunder is a natural non-verbal indication of rain; the word 'thunder' is a conventional verbal indication.
Many indications are mixed; this classification helps analyze them.
4
Classify real Islamic examples
Apply the classification: the word 'zakat' is a conventional verbal indication of the obligatory alms; the sighting of the new moon is a natural non-verbal indication of the start of Ramadan; the design of the universe is a rational non-verbal indication of a Creator.
Think of examples from Quran, hadith, and nature.

Priority of Conceptions over Assents
Author explains why concepts precede assents and resolves a self-refutation objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why concepts must precede assents in logical ordering, and resolve the apparent self-refutation of the proposition 'the absolutely unknown cannot be judged' by distinguishing essential and accidental knowledge.

Core Idea
Concepts are prior because you cannot judge what you absolutely do not know.

This lesson addresses why logicians place concepts (tasawwurat) before assents (tasdiqat). The author argues that concepts are naturally prior because one cannot form a judgment about something absolutely unknown. An apparent paradox arises when the statement 'the absolutely unknown cannot be judged' is itself a judgment about the unknown. The resolution lies in distinguishing between knowledge by essence (the concept) and knowledge by accident (the specific reality).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ordering principle
Understand that the author puts concepts first due to natural priority (tab'an). Ask: Why must concepts be prior? Because judgment requires something known.
2
Analyze the objection
The proposition 'the absolutely unknown cannot be judged' seems to judge the unknown, which would be a judgment about it, contradiction. Pinpoint the problem.
3
Examine the resolution
The author distinguishes two aspects: known by essence (bi al-dhāt) and absolutely unknown by accident (bi al-'arad). The absolute unknown is known as a concept (it has a mental definition) but unknown in its concrete reality. So the judgment is about the concept, not the unknown reality.
4
Apply the distinction
Relate to the earlier example of the unknown face (mentioned in commentary). Show how this distinction resolves similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التَّصَوُّراتُ قَدَّمْنَاهَا وَضْعًا لِتَقَدُّمِهَا طَبَعًا، فَإِنَّ الْمَجْهُولَ الْمُطْلَقَ يَمْتَنِعُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ. قِيلَ: فِيهِ حُكْمٌ فَهُوَ كَذِبٌ، وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّهُ مَعْلُومٌ بِالذَّاتِ، وَمَجْهُولٌ مُطْلَقٌ بِالْعَرَضِ، فَالْحُكْمُ وَسَلْبُهُ بِالِاعْتِبَارَيْنِ، وَسَيَأْتِي.
We have placed the concepts (taṣawwurāt) first in presentation due to their natural priority. For the absolutely unknown cannot be judged. It is said: there is a judgment in it, so it is false. And its resolution: that it is known by essence, and absolutely unknown by accident, so judgment and its negation are by the two considerations, and it will come later.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage explains the order of treatment in logic: concepts (taṣawwurāt) are presented before assents (taṣdīqāt) because concepts are naturally prior. The reason is that one cannot make a judgment (ḥukm) about something that is absolutely unknown. A potential objection is raised: if one says 'the absolutely unknown cannot be judged,' that statement itself is a judgment about the unknown, which seems contradictory. The author resolves this by distinguishing two considerations: the unknown is known in essence (bi al-dhāt) – i.e., as a concept – but unknown in its details by accident. So the judgment that it cannot be judged is about the concept of the unknown, not about its specific reality. Thus, the absolute unknown is only accidentally unknown. This mirrors the earlier argument about the unknown face.

Key Terms
التَّصَوُّرَاتُ
الْمَجْهُولُ الْمُطْلَقُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because assents are more important.
B) Because the absolutely unknown can be judged.
C) Because concepts are naturally prior; one cannot judge about the absolutely unknown.
D) Because the author chose an arbitrary order.

Key Takeaway
Concepts are logically prior to assents because judgment presupposes some knowledge; the absolute unknown is known by essence as a concept, resolving the apparent self-refutation.

Verbal Definition is Conceptual
Author argues that verbal definition is conceptual, not assertive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conceptual demands from assertive demands and identify verbal definition as a conceptual inquiry.

Core Idea
Verbal definition answers 'what is it?' without judgment – it is conceptual, not assertive.

In this passage, the author clarifies a foundational distinction in Islamic logic: the difference between conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Verbal definition, he argues, belongs to the former, as it merely clarifies a concept without making a claim that can be true or false. This is crucial for understanding the scope of logical inquiry and avoiding confusion with linguistic investigations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question type
When an inquiry is presented, determine whether the core question is 'what is it?' (mā) or 'is it?' (hal). The author states that verbal definition answers 'mā'.
The Arabic 'mā' seeks a concept; 'hal' seeks a judgment.
2
Check for judgment
If the answer only explains the meaning of a term without asserting a truth-value (positive or negative), it is conceptual. The author's example: explaining 'al-ghadanfar' as 'the lion' involves no judgment.
Judgment (hukm) requires a predicate that can be affirmed or denied.
3
Distinguish verbal definition from linguistic inquiry
Linguistic inquiry (bahth lughawi) asks whether a word is used for a meaning; this is assertive because it claims a fact about language. Verbal definition, in contrast, simply provides the concept.
The author warns against conflating these two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّانِي : التَّعْرِيفُ اللَّفْظِيُّ مِنَ الْمَطَالِبِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ ; فَإِنَّهُ جَوَابُ «مَا» ، وَكُلُّ مَا هُوَ جَوَابُ «مَا» فَهُوَ تَصَوُّرٌ، أَلَا تَرَى إِذَا قُلْنَا: الْغَضَنْفَرُ مَوْجُودٌ، فَقَالَ الْمُخَاطَبُ مَا الْغَضَنْفَرُ ؟ فَفَسَّرْنَاهُ بِالْأَسَدِ، فَلَيْسَ هُنَاكَ حُكْمٌ. نَعَمْ، بَيَانُ مَوْضُوعِيَّةِ اللَّفْظِ فِي جَوَابِ هَلْ هَذَا اللَّفْظُ مَوْضُوعٌ لِمَعْنَى بَحْثٌ لَفْظِيٌّ ، يُقْصَدُ إِثْبَاتُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فِي عِلْمِ اللُّغَةِ، فَمَنْ قَالَ إِنَّهُ مِنَ الْمَطَالِبِ التَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ لَمْ يُفَرِّقُ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الْبَحْثِ اللفظي اللُّغَوِي.
The second: verbal definition is among conceptual demands, for it is an answer to 'what' (mā), and everything that is an answer to 'what' is a conceptualization. Do you not see that if we say: 'al-ghadanfar exists', and the interlocutor says: 'What is al-ghadanfar?', and we explain it as 'the lion', then there is no judgment. Yes, the explanation of the lexical usage in answer to 'is this word used for a meaning?' is a verbal inquiry intended to be proven by evidence in the science of language. So whoever says that it is among assertive demands has not distinguished between it and the linguistic verbal inquiry.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage argues that verbal definition (ta'rif lafzi) belongs to conceptual demands (al-matalib al-tasawwuriyya), not assertive demands (al-matalib al-tasdiqiyya). The author explains that verbal definition answers the question 'what is it?' (ma), and such answers are conceptualizations (tasawwurat), not judgments. For example, if someone says 'al-ghadanfar exists' and asks 'what is al-ghadanfar?', explaining it as 'the lion' involves no judgment (hukm). However, there is a different type of inquiry: explaining whether a word is used for a particular meaning is a linguistic verbal inquiry (bahth lafzi lughawi) that does involve proof and is thus assertive. Those who confuse verbal definition with linguistic inquiry are mistaken.

Key Terms
المطالب التصورية
المطالب التصديقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is verbal definition considered a conceptual demand?
A) Because it involves a judgment about existence
B) Because it answers 'what is it?' and involves no judgment
C) Because it requires evidence from linguistics
D) Because it is a type of assertive inquiry

Key Takeaway
The essential logical point: verbal definition provides a concept without judgment, placing it in the realm of tasawwur, not tasdiq.

Total vs. Detailed Judgment and the Three Parts of a Proposition
Author distinguishes total and detailed judgment, and identifies the three parts of a proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish total judgment from detailed judgment and identify the three component parts of a proposition.

Core Idea
Judgment is either intuitive unity or analytic division; the relation is subordinate.

This passage dissects the nature of judgment and proposition in Islamic logic. It distinguishes between total judgment (an immediate, holistic grasp) and detailed judgment (the analytic process that logic studies). It then clarifies that the relation between subject and predicate is not an independent part but a dependent mirror, and finally identifies the three essential components of a proposition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two types of judgment
The author divides judgment into total (ijmali) and detailed (tafsili). Total judgment is an immediate grasp of unity between two things; detailed judgment is the logical one requiring multiple distinct forms.
Total judgment is pre-analytical; detailed judgment is the object of logic.
2
Analyze the status of the relation (nisba)
The relation enters judgment only as subordinate. It is a meaning of a particle (ma'na harfi) that cannot be considered independently; it is a mirror for the state of the two sides.
The relation is not an independent term but a linking element.
3
Identify the three parts of a proposition
A proposition (qadiyya) is completed by three things: subject, predicate, and a complete predicative relation (nisba tamma ikhbariyya) that reports.
The relation is the third part, not the judgment itself.
4
Derive the nature of opinion (zann)
Because the relation is not an independent part, opinion is a simple assent. If the relation were a separate part, the proposition would have four parts, contradicting the analysis.
This shows the logical structure is minimal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْحُكْمُ مِنْهُ إِجْمَالِيُّ ، وَهُوَ الْكِشَافُ الإِنْحَادِ بَيْنَ الْأَمْرَيْنِ دَفْعَةٌ وَاحِدَةً، وَمِنْهُ تَفْصِيلِي، وَهُوَ الْمَنْطِقِيُّ الَّذِي يَسْتَدْعِي صُوَرًا مُتَعَدِّدَةً مُفَصَّلَةٌ ، وَالنِّسْبَةُ إِنَّمَا تَدْخُلُ فِي مُتَعَلَّقِ الْحُكْمِ بِالتَّبَعِيَّةِ ؛ لأَنَّهَا مِنَ الْمَعَانِي الْحَرْفِيَّةِ الَّتِي لَا تُلَاحَظُ بِالِاسْتِقْلَالِ ، إِنَّمَا هِيَ مِرْآةٌ لِمُلَاحَظَةِ حَالِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، بَلْ إِنَّمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ الْحُكْمُ حَقِيقَةٌ بِمُفَادِ الْهَيْئَةِ التَّرْكِيبيَّةِ ، وَهُوَ الاِتِّحَادُ مَثَلًا، فَتَدَبَّر . ثُمَّ الْقَضِيَّةُ إِنَّمَا تَتِمُّ بِأُمُورٍ ثَلَاثَةٍ ، ثَالِتُهَا نِسْبَةٌ تَامَّةٌ إِخْبَارِيَّةٌ حَاكِيَةٌ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا يَسْتَبِينُ أَنَّ الظَّنَّ إِذْعَانُ بَسِيطٌ ، وَإِلَّا لَصَارَ أَجْزَاءُ الْقَضِيَّةِ هُنَاكَ أَرْبَعَةٌ.
Judgment is from it total, which is the revealing of the unity between the two things at once; and from it detailed, which is the logical one that requires multiple detailed forms. The relation enters into the object of judgment by way of subordination, because it is from the aspectual meanings that are not considered independently; rather, it is a mirror for observing the state of the two sides. Rather, judgment in reality attaches to the import of the compositional structure, which is unity, for example. So reflect. Then the proposition is only completed by three things, the third of which is a complete predicative relation that reports. And from here it becomes clear that opinion is a simple assent; otherwise, the parts of the proposition would there be four.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage discusses assents (al-tasdiqat). It begins by distinguishing two types of judgment (hukm): total (ijmali), which is an immediate intuitive grasp of the unity between two things, and detailed (tafsili), which is the logical judgment that involves multiple detailed forms (i.e., analytic). The relation (nisba) between subject and predicate enters the judgment only as a dependent (bi al-taba'iyya), because it is a meaning of a particle (ma'na harfi) that is not considered independently; it is like a mirror that reflects the state of the two sides. In reality, judgment attaches to the import of the compositional structure (hay'a tarkibiyya), such as unity. Then the author states that a proposition (qadiyya) is completed by three things: subject, predicate, and a complete predicative relation (nisba tamma ikhbariyya hakiya). From this it becomes clear that opinion (zann) is a simple assent (idh'an basit); otherwise, if the relation were considered an independent part, the proposition would have four parts instead of three.

Key Terms
الحكم
الإجمالي والتفصيلي
النسبة
القضية
الظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, into how many parts does a proposition (qadiyya) necessarily divide?
A) Two: subject and predicate.
B) Three: subject, predicate, and relation.
C) Four: subject, predicate, relation, and judgment.
D) Five: including qualification and modality.

Key Takeaway
Total judgment is intuitive unity; detailed judgment is analytic; the relation is a dependent mirror, not an independent part, keeping the proposition at three components.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Prayer is obligatory.' Determine whether the judgment 'Prayer is obligatory' is total or detailed. Then list the three component parts of this proposition.
?
Explain why the relation 'is' in the proposition 'The world is created' is not an independent part of the judgment, using the author's argument about the relation being a mirror.

Critique of the Later Logicians' Theory of Doubt
Author critiques later logicians' view that doubt attaches to relational aspect while assent attaches to occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's objection to the later logicians' theory of doubt and explain why he insists doubt must involve occurrence.

Core Idea
Doubt must involve the occurrence itself, not just the relational aspect.

In this passage, the author challenges the later logicians' distinction between doubt (shakk) attaching to the relational aspect (nisba taqayyudiyya) and assent (tasdiq) attaching to occurrence. He argues that hesitation necessarily concerns occurrence, and the perceived object is the same in both doubt and certainty. Let's dissect his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the later logicians' claim
Locate where the author states that later scholars claimed doubt attaches to the relational aspect (al-nisba al-taqayyudiyya), while judgment of occurrence only receives assent.
2
Note the author's astonishment
The author asks: 'Did they not understand that hesitation does not constitute a reality unless it attaches to occurrence?' This is the key objection.
3
Recognize the author's positive claim
He asserts that the perceived object in both doubt and certainty is the same; the difference is only in the mode of perception (assentive vs. hesitative). Therefore the earlier scholars are correct.
4
Follow the further doubt and resolution
The author raises a potential problem: in doubt, all three parts of the proposition are present, yet according to the well-known view they are not realized. He examines and rejects a proposed solution involving accidental whole, concluding that the proposition is a proposition in all cases because it conveys a meaning that admits truth and falsehood. Hesitation is only about correspondence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُتَأَخِّرُونَ زَعَمُوا أَنَّ الشَّكَ مُتَعَلَّقٌ بِالنِّسْبَةِ التَّقَيُّدِيَّةِ ، وَهِيَ مَوْرِدُ الْحُكْمِ ، وَيُسَمُّونَهَا النَّسْبَةَ بَيْنَ بَيْنَ، وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ بِمَعْنَى الْوُقُوعِ وَاللَّاوُقُوعِ فَلَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ إِلَّا التَّصْدِيقُ أَعْجَبَنِي قَوْلُهُمْ، أَمَا فَهِمُوا أَنَّ التَّرَدُّدَ لَا يَتَقَوَّمُ حَقِيقَةٌ مَا لَمْ يَتَعَلَّقَ بِالْوُقُوعِ ؟ فَالْمُدْرَكُ فِي الصُّورَتَيْنِ وَاحِدٌ ، وَالتَّفَاوُتُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الْإِدْرَاكِ بِأَنَّهُ إِذْعَانِيُّ أَوْ تَرَدُّدِيُّ ، فَقَوْلُ الْقُدَمَاءِ هُوَ الْحَقُّ .
وَهَهُنَا شَكٌ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الْمَعْلُومَاتِ الثَّلاثَةَ الَّتِي هِيَ جَمِيعُ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ مُتَحَقِّقَةٌ فى صُورَةِ الشَّكُ، مَعَ أَنَّهَا غَيْرُ مُتَحَقِّقَةٍ عَلَى مَا هُوَ الْمَشْهُورُ .
قِيلَ فِي حَلِّهِ: إِنَّ الْقَضِيَّةَ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى تِلْكَ الْمَعْلُومَاتِ كُلّ بِالْعَرَضِ ، فَلاَ يَلْزَمُ تَحَقُّقُهُ ، كَالْكَاتِبِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْحَيَوَانِ النَّاطِقِ. أَقُولُ: إِذَا كَانَ كُلاً بِالْعَرَضِ فَيَجِبُ أَنْ يُعْتَبَرَ أَمْرُ آخَرُ بَعْدَ الْوُقُوعِ ، وَلَيْسَ إِلَّا إِدْرَاكُهُ، وَذَلِكَ خَارِجٌ إِجْمَاعًا. وَأَخُذُ الْوُقُوعِ بِشَرْطِ الْإِيقَاعِ تَصْحِيحٌ لِمَجْعُولِيَّةِ الذَّاتِي، وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ .
وَالْإِفَادَةُ مُقَدَّمٌ عَلَى الْإِيقَاعِ ، وَالْقَضِيَّةُ لَيْسَتْ مُنْتَظِرَةَ التَّحْصِيلِ بَعْدَهَا، فَاعْتِبَارُ تَعَلَّقِ الْإِيفَاعِ بِالْوُقُوعِ مِمَّا لَا دَخَلَ لَهُ في تَحْصِيلِ هَذِهِ الْحَقِيقَةِ، فَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ قَوْلَنَا زَيْدٌ هُوَ قَائِمٌ قَضِيَّةٌ عَلَى كُلِّ تَقْدِيرٍ؛ فَإِنَّهُ يُفِيدُ مَعْنَى مُحْتَمِلًا لِلصِّدْقِ وَالْكَذِبِ، فَفِي الشَّكْ إِنَّمَا التَّرَدُّدُ في مُطَابَقَةِ الْحِكَايَةِ، لَا فِي أَصْلِ الْحِكَايَةِ وَاحْتِمَالِهَا لَهُمَا .
نَعَمْ ، الْقَضَايَا الْمُعْتَبَرَةُ فِي الْعُلُومِ هِيَ الَّتِي تَعَلَّقَ بِهَا الْإِذْعَانُ ; إِذْ لَا كَمَالَ في تَحْصِيلِ الشَّكَ . هَذَا ، وَإِنْ كَانَ مِمَّا لَمْ يَقْرَعُ سَمْعَكَ ، لَكِنَّهُ هُوَ التَّحْقِيقُ .
And the later scholars claimed that doubt is attached to the relational aspect, and it is the locus of judgment, and they call it the relation 'between-between'. As for judgment in the sense of occurrence and non-occurrence, only assent attaches to it. I admire their statement: Did they not understand that hesitation does not constitute a reality unless it attaches to occurrence? So the perceived in the two forms is one, and the difference is only in the perception, whether it is assentive or hesitative. So the statement of the earlier scholars is the truth. And here is a doubt: that the three cognitions which are all the parts of the proposition are realized in the form of doubt, despite them being unrealized according to what is well-known. It was said in its resolution: The proposition, in relation to those cognitions, is an accidental whole, so its realization is not necessary, like the writer in relation to the rational animal. I say: If it is an accidental whole, then another matter after occurrence must be considered, and it is nothing but its apprehension, and that is outside by consensus. And taking occurrence with the condition of occurrence-positing is a validation of the artificiality of the essential, and it is impossible. And the signification is prior to the positing of occurrence, and the proposition does not await attainment after it. So considering the attachment of occurrence-positing to occurrence has no role in attaining this reality. So the truth is that our statement 'Zayd is standing' is a proposition in any case; for it conveys a meaning that admits truth and falsehood. So in doubt, the hesitation is only about the correspondence of the report, not about the report itself and its potential for both. Yes, the propositions considered in the sciences are those to which conviction attaches, since there is no perfection in attaining doubt. This, even if it has not reached your ears, is the verification.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological critique of the later logicians' theory of doubt. The author begins by stating the later scholars' claim: doubt (shakk) attaches to the relational aspect (al-nisba al-taqayyudiyya), which they call the 'between-between' relation, while judgment as occurrence/non-occurrence only receives assent (tasdiq). The author finds this astonishing, arguing that hesitation (taraddud) necessarily involves the occurrence (wuqu') itself. The perceived object in both certainty and doubt is the same; the difference is only in the mode of perception—whether it is assenting (idh'ani) or hesitating. Thus the earlier scholars are correct. Then the author raises a further doubt: in a state of doubt, the three components of the proposition (subject, predicate, relation) are all present in the mind, yet according to the well-known view they are not realized. A proposed solution says the proposition is an accidental whole in relation to those components, so its realization is not necessary—like 'writer' in relation to 'rational animal'. The author rejects this: if it is accidental, then an additional factor after occurrence is needed, which is apprehension, but that is external by consensus. Taking occurrence with the condition of 'occurence-positing' (iqa') is an invalid attempt to make the essential artificial. The author insists that signification (ifada) precedes occurence-positing, and the proposition is not awaiting attainment after it. Hence the proposition 'Zayd is standing' is a proposition in all cases because it conveys a meaning that can be true or false. In doubt, the hesitation is about whether the report corresponds to reality, not about the report itself or its capacity for truth/falsehood. Finally, the author notes that only propositions with conviction are considered in the sciences, as there is no benefit in merely attaining doubt.

Key Terms
الشَّك
النِّسْبَةُ التَّقَيُّدِيَّةُ
التَّصْدِيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the later logicians' claim about doubt and assent mistaken?
A) Because doubt cannot attach to the relational aspect
B) Because hesitation necessarily involves occurrence, not just the relation
C) Because assent is not about occurrence
D) Because the relational aspect is not a real aspect

Key Takeaway
Doubt is not merely about the relational aspect of a proposition; it necessarily concerns the occurrence of that relation, making the difference between doubt and assent one of mode of perception, not object.

How to Judge Impossible Objects: The Nature-Based Account
Author presents his doctrine on judgments about impossible objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the impossible has no mental form and how judgments about impossibility are correctly understood as applying to natures (tabi'a) with respect to their instances.

Core Idea
Impossible objects have no mental existence; judgments of impossibility are about natures, not individuals.

This passage addresses a subtle but crucial point in Islamic logic: how can we legitimately make propositions like 'A partner of the Creator is impossible' if the subject has no existence? The author clarifies that such judgments are about the universal nature considered under the aspect of its non-instantiability.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the status of the impossible
The impossible has no form in the mind or outside; it is absolutely non-existent.
2
Understand the constraint on judgment
Every mental existent is real in nafs al-amr. So we cannot judge the impossible itself; we judge the conceived nature.
3
Identify the subject of judgment
The subject is the nature (tabi'a) conceived, not an individual.
4
Apply to examples
See how 'Partner of the Creator is impossible' works: the nature 'partner of the Creator' has no instances, so impossibility is attributed to that nature.
5
Distinguish from improper judgments
Contrast with judgments on individuals, which the author rejects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّانِيَةُ : الْمُحَالُ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ مُحَالٌ لَيْسَ لَهُ صُورَةٌ فِي الْعَقْلِ، فَهُوَ مَعْدُومٌ ذِهْنَا وَخَارِجًا، وَمِنْ هَهُنَا تَبَيَّنَ أَنَّ كُلَّ مَوْجُودٍ في الذِّهْنِ حَقِيقَةٌ مَوْجُودٌ في نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، فَلَا يُحْكَمُ عَلَيْهِ إِيجَابًا بِالامْتِنَاعِ ، أَوْ سَلْبًا بِالْوُجُودِ مَثَلًا، إِلَّا عَلَى أَمْرٍ كُلى، إذَا كَانَ مِنَ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ تَصَوُّرُهُ. وَكُلُّ مَحْكُومٍ عَلَيْهِ بِالتَّحْقِيقِ هِيَ الطَّبِيعَةُ الْمُتَصَوَّرَةُ، وَكُلُّ مُتَصَوَّرٍ ثَابِتٌ ، فَلَا يَصحُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ هُوَ بِالامْتِنَاعِ وَمَا يَحْذُو حَذْوَهُ. نَعَمْ، إِذَا لُوحِظَ بِاعْتِبَارِ جَمِيعِ مَوَارِدِ تَحَقُّقِهِ أَوْ بَعْضِهَا يَصِحُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ بِالِامْتِنَاعِ مَثَلًا، فَالِامْتِنَاعُ ثَابِتُ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ، وَذَلِكَ صَادِقٌ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوَارِدِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ لَا إِشْكَالَ بِالْقَضَايَا الَّتِي مَحْمُولَاتُهَا مُنَافِيَةٌ لِلْوُجُودِ، نَحْو: «شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُمْتَنِع»، وَاجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ مُحَالٌ، وَالْمَجْهُولُ الْمُطْلَقُ يَمْتَنِعُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ ، وَالْمَعْدُومُ الْمُطْلَقُ يُقَابِلُ الْمَوْجُودَ الْمُطْلَقَ . وَأَمَّا الَّذينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الْأَفْرَادِ حَقِيقَةٌ فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ: إِنَّهَا سَوَالِبُ، وَلَا رَيْبَ أَنَّهُ تَحَكُمْ. وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ إِنَّهَا مُوجَبَاتٌ، لَا تَقْتَضِي إِلَّا تَصَوُّرَ الْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ حَالَ الْحُكْمِ ، كَمَا فِي السَّوَالِبِ مِنْ غَيْرِ فَرْقٍ، وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يُصَادِمُ الْبَدَاهَةَ . وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ: إِنَّ الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الْأَفْرَادِ الْفَرْضِيَّةِ الْمُقَدَّرَةِ الْوُجُودِ، كَأَنَّهُ قَالَ مَثَلاً: مَا يُتَصَوَّرُ بِعِنْوَانِ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي ، وَيُفْرَضُ صِدْقُهُ عَلَيْهِ مُمْتَنع في نَفْس الأمر. وَلاَ يَذْهَبُ عَلَيْكَ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ يَكُونَ ثُبُوتُ الصِّفَةِ أَزْيَدَ مِنْ تُبُوتِ الْمَوْصُوفِ ; فَإِنَّ الاِمْتِنَاعَ مُتَحَقِّقُ وَثَابِتُ في نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، بِخِلَافِ الْأَفْرَادِ، فَتَدَبَّرُ.
The second [subtlety]: The impossible insofar as it is impossible has no form in the mind, so it is non-existent mentally and externally. From here it becomes clear that every existent in the mind is truly existent in reality (nafs al-amr). So one cannot judge it affirmatively with impossibility or negatively with existence, for example, except on a universal matter if its conception is among the possibilities. And everything judged with verification is the conceived nature, and every conceived thing is established. So it is not valid to judge it as such with impossibility and similar. Yes, if it is considered with regard to all or some of its instances of actualization, then it is valid to judge it with impossibility, for example. Thus impossibility is established for the nature, and that is true by the non-existence of instances, and then there is no problem with propositions whose predicates are incompatible with existence, like 'Partner of the Creator is impossible,' 'The conjunction of two contradictories is impossible,' 'The absolutely unknown cannot be judged,' 'The absolutely non-existent is opposite to the absolutely existent.' As for those who said that the judgment on individuals is true: some said they are negatives, but that is arbitration without doubt. Some said they are affirmatives that only require conception of the subject at the time of judgment like negatives, and it is obvious that this contradicts intuition. Some said that judgment is on assumed hypothetical individuals of existence, as if he said, for example: what is conceived under the description of 'partner of the Creator' and its being assumed true of it is impossible in reality. And do not overlook that the establishment of the attribute must be additional to the establishment of the described; for impossibility is actual and established in reality, unlike the individuals. So ponder.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage addresses judgments about impossible objects. It begins by stating that the impossible as such has no mental image, so it is non-existent both mentally and externally. Every mental existent is real in nafs al-amr. Therefore, one cannot judge an impossible thing as impossible directly; rather, judgment applies to the conceived nature (tabi'a) when considered with respect to all or some of its instances. For example, 'Partner of the Creator is impossible' is true because the nature 'partner of the Creator' has no instances. The author then critiques three alternative views held by those who think judgment is about individuals: (1) that they are negative propositions – this is arbitrary; (2) that they are affirmative but require only conception – this contradicts intuition; (3) that they are about hypothetical individuals assumed to exist – this confuses the real status of impossibility. The author concludes by noting that impossibility is a real attribute established in reality, whereas the supposed individuals are not.

Key Terms
الْمُحَال (al-muhal): The impossible; that which cannot exist in any possible world.
نَفْس الأمْر (nafs al-amr): Reality; the external world independent of the mind.
طَبِيعَة (tabi'a): Nature; the universal essence considered irrespective of existence.
مَوَارِد (mawarid): Instances/individuals; the concrete cases where a nature is realized.
الْأَفْرَاد (al-afrad): Individuals; the particular entities that fall under a universal.
سَوَالِب (sawalib): Negative propositions; propositions of the form 'S is not P'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct way to understand the proposition 'A partner of the Creator is impossible'?
A) It is a judgment about a specific individual that exists in the mind.
B) It is a judgment about the nature (tabi'a) of a partner of the Creator, considered with respect to all its instances.
C) It is a judgment about a hypothetical individual assumed to exist.
D) It is a negative proposition that does not require a subject.

Key Takeaway
The impossible has no mental existence, so judgments of impossibility are correctly analyzed as predications on the nature with respect to the non-existence of its instances.

?
Consider the kalam proposition 'The world is temporally originated (hadith).' Explain whether this is a judgment about an individual world or about the nature of 'the world' in your view based on the author's criteria.

The Refutation of Alternative Views on Judging Individuals
Author refutes three alternative views on the subject of judgments about impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three alternative views on judging individuals critiqued by the author and explain why each fails.

Core Idea
Three attempts to ground judgments on individuals all fail: arbitrarily, against intuition, or by confusing ontological levels.

After establishing his own account, the author forcefully criticizes three alternative positions held by those who believe judgment is about individuals. Each is shown to be untenable. This lesson unpacks those critiques and reinforces the correct view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first alternative
Some say the propositions are negative (sawalib). The author dismisses this as arbitrary (tahakkum).
The passage says: 'وَلَا رَيْبَ أَنَّهُ تَحَكُمْ' meaning 'there is no doubt that it is arbitration (arbitrary).'
2
Identify the second alternative
Some say they are affirmatives requiring only conception of the subject. The author says this contradicts intuition (badaha).
The passage says: 'وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يُصَادِمُ الْبَدَاهَةَ' meaning 'and it is obvious that it contradicts intuition.'
3
Identify the third alternative
Some say judgment is on hypothetical individuals assumed to exist. The author points out that impossibility is real while individuals are not, so the attribute would be more real than the subject.
The passage argues that the attribute (impossibility) is established in reality, but the individuals (the hypothetical subjects) are not, leading to an absurdity.
4
Assess the author's reasoning
For each alternative, note the type of objection: logical, intuitive, ontological.
5
Reinforce the correct view
Connect back to the nature-based account: judgments about impossibility are about natures, not individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّانِيَةُ : الْمُحَالُ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ مُحَالٌ لَيْسَ لَهُ صُورَةٌ فِي الْعَقْلِ، فَهُوَ مَعْدُومٌ ذِهْنَا وَخَارِجًا، وَمِنْ هَهُنَا تَبَيَّنَ أَنَّ كُلَّ مَوْجُودٍ في الذِّهْنِ حَقِيقَةٌ مَوْجُودٌ في نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، فَلَا يُحْكَمُ عَلَيْهِ إِيجَابًا بِالامْتِنَاعِ ، أَوْ سَلْبًا بِالْوُجُودِ مَثَلًا، إِلَّا عَلَى أَمْرٍ كُلى، إذَا كَانَ مِنَ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ تَصَوُّرُهُ. وَكُلُّ مَحْكُومٍ عَلَيْهِ بِالتَّحْقِيقِ هِيَ الطَّبِيعَةُ الْمُتَصَوَّرَةُ، وَكُلُّ مُتَصَوَّرٍ ثَابِتٌ ، فَلَا يَصحُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ هُوَ بِالامْتِنَاعِ وَمَا يَحْذُو حَذْوَهُ. نَعَمْ، إِذَا لُوحِظَ بِاعْتِبَارِ جَمِيعِ مَوَارِدِ تَحَقُّقِهِ أَوْ بَعْضِهَا يَصِحُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ بِالِامْتِنَاعِ مَثَلًا، فَالِامْتِنَاعُ ثَابِتُ لِلطَّبِيعَةِ، وَذَلِكَ صَادِقٌ بِانْتِفَاءِ الْمَوَارِدِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ لَا إِشْكَالَ بِالْقَضَايَا الَّتِي مَحْمُولَاتُهَا مُنَافِيَةٌ لِلْوُجُودِ، نَحْو: «شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي مُمْتَنِع»، وَاجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ مُحَالٌ، وَالْمَجْهُولُ الْمُطْلَقُ يَمْتَنِعُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ ، وَالْمَعْدُومُ الْمُطْلَقُ يُقَابِلُ الْمَوْجُودَ الْمُطْلَقَ . وَأَمَّا الَّذينَ قَالُوا إِنَّ الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الْأَفْرَادِ حَقِيقَةٌ فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ: إِنَّهَا سَوَالِبُ، وَلَا رَيْبَ أَنَّهُ تَحَكُمْ. وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ إِنَّهَا مُوجَبَاتٌ، لَا تَقْتَضِي إِلَّا تَصَوُّرَ الْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ حَالَ الْحُكْمِ ، كَمَا فِي السَّوَالِبِ مِنْ غَيْرِ فَرْقٍ، وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يُصَادِمُ الْبَدَاهَةَ . وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ: إِنَّ الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الْأَفْرَادِ الْفَرْضِيَّةِ الْمُقَدَّرَةِ الْوُجُودِ، كَأَنَّهُ قَالَ مَثَلاً: مَا يُتَصَوَّرُ بِعِنْوَانِ شَرِيكِ الْبَارِي ، وَيُفْرَضُ صِدْقُهُ عَلَيْهِ مُمْتَنع في نَفْس الأمر. وَلاَ يَذْهَبُ عَلَيْكَ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ أَنْ يَكُونَ ثُبُوتُ الصِّفَةِ أَزْيَدَ مِنْ تُبُوتِ الْمَوْصُوفِ ; فَإِنَّ الاِمْتِنَاعَ مُتَحَقِّقُ وَثَابِتُ في نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، بِخِلَافِ الْأَفْرَادِ، فَتَدَبَّرُ.
The second [subtlety]: The impossible insofar as it is impossible has no form in the mind, so it is non-existent mentally and externally. From here it becomes clear that every existent in the mind is truly existent in reality (nafs al-amr). So one cannot judge it affirmatively with impossibility or negatively with existence, for example, except on a universal matter if its conception is among the possibilities. And everything judged with verification is the conceived nature, and every conceived thing is established. So it is not valid to judge it as such with impossibility and similar. Yes, if it is considered with regard to all or some of its instances of actualization, then it is valid to judge it with impossibility, for example. Thus impossibility is established for the nature, and that is true by the non-existence of instances, and then there is no problem with propositions whose predicates are incompatible with existence, like 'Partner of the Creator is impossible,' 'The conjunction of two contradictories is impossible,' 'The absolutely unknown cannot be judged,' 'The absolutely non-existent is opposite to the absolutely existent.' As for those who said that the judgment on individuals is true: some said they are negatives, but that is arbitration without doubt. Some said they are affirmatives that only require conception of the subject at the time of judgment like negatives, and it is obvious that this contradicts intuition. Some said that judgment is on assumed hypothetical individuals of existence, as if he said, for example: what is conceived under the description of 'partner of the Creator' and its being assumed true of it is impossible in reality. And do not overlook that the establishment of the attribute must be additional to the establishment of the described; for impossibility is actual and established in reality, unlike the individuals. So ponder.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage addresses judgments about impossible objects. It begins by stating that the impossible as such has no mental image, so it is non-existent both mentally and externally. Every mental existent is real in nafs al-amr. Therefore, one cannot judge an impossible thing as impossible directly; rather, judgment applies to the conceived nature (tabi'a) when considered with respect to all or some of its instances. For example, 'Partner of the Creator is impossible' is true because the nature 'partner of the Creator' has no instances. The author then critiques three alternative views held by those who think judgment is about individuals: (1) that they are negative propositions – this is arbitrary; (2) that they are affirmative but require only conception – this contradicts intuition; (3) that they are about hypothetical individuals assumed to exist – this confuses the real status of impossibility. The author concludes by noting that impossibility is a real attribute established in reality, whereas the supposed individuals are not.

Key Terms
الْمُحَال (al-muhal): The impossible; that which cannot exist in any possible world.
نَفْس الأمْر (nafs al-amr): Reality; the external world independent of the mind.
طَبِيعَة (tabi'a): Nature; the universal essence considered irrespective of existence.
مَوَارِد (mawarid): Instances/individuals; the concrete cases where a nature is realized.
الْأَفْرَاد (al-afrad): Individuals; the particular entities that fall under a universal.
سَوَالِب (sawalib): Negative propositions; propositions of the form 'S is not P'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It contradicts intuition.
B) It is arbitrary (tahakkum).
C) It confuses nature and individual.
D) It requires conception of the subject.

Key Takeaway
The author's critique shows that any attempt to base judgments on individual impossible objects leads to insoluble problems, affirming the correct nature-based account.

The Paradox of the Set of All Concepts and Its Resolution
Author poses a doubt about the set of all concepts and resolves the resulting paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the paradoxical implication that arises when considering the set of all concepts and explain the resolution provided in the text.

Core Idea
A part cannot be the contradictory of the whole, so assuming a complete set of all concepts leads to an absurdity.

This passage presents a subtle logical doubt: if we try to take all concepts together such that nothing escapes, then the removal of that set becomes its contradictory. But that removal is itself a concept, so it must be included in the set, leading to the absurd conclusion that a part (the removal) is the contradictory of the whole set. The author resolves this by noting that the consideration of concepts does not stop at a limit—assuming a complete set requires a limit, which contradicts the unlimited nature of concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assumption
The doubt begins with the assumption that we can take all concepts ('جميع المفاهيم') such that nothing escapes, i.e., a complete and exhaustive set of all concepts.
This is the initial premise that leads to the paradox.
2
Extract the contradictory
If we remove (رفع) this set, the removal is logically its contradictory. But that removal is itself a concept, so it must be included within the set of all concepts.
This creates a self-referential inclusion problem.
3
Recognize the absurd conclusion
The text concludes that 'the part is the contradictory of the whole' (فَالْجُزْءُ نَقِيضُ الْكُلِّ), which is logically impossible. A part cannot be the contradictory of the whole.
This is the reductio ad absurdum that exposes the flaw in the initial assumption.
4
Understand the resolution
The author resolves the paradox by stating that the consideration of concepts does not stop at a limit (لا يقف عند حد), while non-increase (عدم الزيادة) would require a limit. Assuming a complete set of all concepts involves considering two mutually exclusive things—the unlimited nature of concepts and the limit required for completeness.
The resolution rejects the coherence of a fully delimited set of all concepts.
5
Reflect on the instruction 'so ponder'
The author ends with 'فتدبر' (so ponder), indicating that the resolution is subtle and requires deep reflection to fully grasp.
This encourages the reader to internalize the logical nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ ، وَهُوَ أَنَّا إِذَا أَخَذْنَا جَمِيعَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ بِحَيْثُ لَا يَشُدُّ عَنْهُ شَيْءٌ، فَرَفْعُهُ نَقِيضُهُ، وَذَلِكَ دَاخِلٌ فِي الْجَمِيع ; فَالْجُزْءُ نَقِيضُ الْكُلِّ ، وَهُوَ مُحالٌ ، وَمِثْلُهُ يُورَدُ عَلَى تَغَابُرِ النَّسْبَةِ لِلْمُنتَسِبَيْنِ. وَحَلَّهُ: أَنَّ اعْتِبَارَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ لَا يَقِفُ عِنْدَ حَدٌ، وَعَدَمُ الزَّيَادَةِ يَقْتَضِي الْوُقُوفَ إِلَى حَدٌ، فَأَخْذُ الْجَمِيعِ كَذَلِكَ اعْتِبَارُ لِلْمُتَنَافِيَيْنِ ، فَتَدَبَّرُ .
And here is a doubt: that if we take all concepts such that nothing escapes it, then its removal is its contradictory, and that is included in the all. So the part is the contradictory of the whole, which is impossible. And similarly it is raised on the distinctness of the relation to the two relatives. Its resolution: that the consideration of concepts does not stop at a limit, and non-increase requires stopping at a limit, so taking all such is considering two contradictory things. So ponder.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents a logical doubt: If we consider the set of all concepts (جميع المفاهيم) such that no concept is excluded, then its removal (رفعه) is its contradictory. But that removal is itself a concept and thus included in the all. This implies that a part (the removal) is the contradictory of the whole (the all), which is absurd. The resolution: considering concepts without limit is not possible, and the requirement of non-increase presupposes a limit, so assuming such an all is actually considering two mutually exclusive things. The resolution is subtle and requires reflection (فتدبر).

Key Terms
مفاهيم
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Concepts cannot be removed.
D) The set of all concepts is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The set of all concepts leads to a paradox that is resolved by recognizing that the consideration of concepts cannot be limited to a closed set.

Three Arguments Against Conceptualizing Allah's Essence
Three logical arguments proving the impossibility of Allah's essence entering the mind

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three logical arguments that prove Allah's essence cannot be represented in the mind and identify their underlying reductio structure.

Core Idea
Allah's essence cannot enter the mind because that would contradict His absolute singularity.

The commentator begins a profound epistemological investigation: can the human mind form a concept of Allah's essence? He answers with a firm no, and supports it with three rigorous arguments from first principles. These arguments are not merely theological assertions but logical demonstrations that guard against any attempt to confine the Divine within mental categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central thesis
The passage asserts that Allah's essence (kunh) cannot occur in the mind. Underline the statement 'لا يدرك الله تعالى بكنهه' and note it as the conclusion to be proved.
2
Extract the first argument
The author supposes the opposite: if Allah's essence did occur in the mind while also remaining external, there would need to be a shared element between mental and external existence, leading to multiplicity and undermining the individuation of the essence. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
Key phrase: 'فلا بد من أمر مشترك بينهما'
3
Extract the second argument
Allah's external existence is identical to His essence without any additional aspect. Therefore, if it occurred in the mind, that mental existence would be subject to both external and mental accidents simultaneously, which is impossible.
Key phrase: 'مصداق الوجود الخارجي عين ذاته تعالى'
4
Extract the third argument
Allah's essence is the very referent of the predicate 'necessary existence'. If it occurred in the mind, it would be both necessary (in itself) and dependent (on the mind as a locus), a contradiction.
Key phrase: 'ذاته مصداق حمل الواجب'
5
Synthesize the conclusion
The author concludes that no particular individual as such can be fully present in the mind. This is a general principle that applies most forcefully to Allah.
The phrase 'الجزئي من حيث هو شخص لا يحصل في الذهن' summarizes the outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الكتاب وجوده إلا أنه ليس خارجا ، حتى يكون مقسما .
ومن جملة تقديساته قوله: (ولا يتصور)، إن قُرِئَ بِضَمَ عَلَامَةِ بيان استحالة الاِسْتِقْبَالِ ، فمعناه لا يدرك الله تعالى بكنهه ؛ لامتناع حصوله في الذهن ، ولا شبح له حتى يحصل، ولو فرض لا يكون مبدأ الانكشاف بالضرورة؛ لأن المباين لا يكون مبدأ للانكشاف لمباين آخر .
وأما امتناع حصوله في الذهن فمن وجوه، الأولن أنه لو حصل في الذهن فحصوله إما مع بقائه في الخارج، فلا بد من أمر مشترك بينهما واجب عن الآخر، فلا يكون المميز الذي هو التعين عينه.
لأحدهما حقيقة ، ومميز وأيضا يلزم تكثر الجزئي أو مع بطلانه في الخارج، وهو أشد استحالة ههنا ظهر أيضا أن الجزئي من حيث هو شخص لا يحصل في الذهن، ولا الحاسة حين الإدراك .
الثاني أن مصداق الوجود الخارجي عين ذاته تعالى، من غير حيثية زائدة ، فلو حصل في الذهن لكان مصداقا للعوارض الخارجية والذهنية معا . الثالث: أن ذاته مصداق حمل الواجب ، بلا حيثية زائدة، فلو حصل في الذهن لكان واجبا ومحتاجا إلى الذهن الذي هو محله.
وههنا برهانان عرشيان على امتناع تصوره بكنهه، الأول: ما أفاده الشيخ الأكبر، والإمام الأعظم، سند الأولياء والأتقياء ، معدن الهداية خاتم فص الولاية، حسنة من حسنات سيد المرسلين الذي كان وليا وآدم بين الماء والطين، الشيخ محيي الملة والدين محمد بن العربي قدس سره، ورضي الله عنه في (الفتوحات المكية): أنه تعالى يخالف المخلوقات، لا مناسبة بينه وبين خلقه البتة ، وكيف لا يشبه من يقبل المثال بمن يقبل المثال ، فالعلم بالله تعالى عزيز عن إدراك العقل والنفس، إلا أنه واجب تعالى وتقدس، وكلما يتلفظ به في حق المخلوقات، أو يتوهم في المركبات وغيرها فالله تعالى في نظر العقل السليم بخلاف ذلك ، لا يجوز عليه الوهم انتهى ملخصا هذا كلام متين ، يعجز عن فهمه إلا من أتى الله بقلب سليم.
الثاني ما نقل عن المعلم الأول : أنه سبحانه جلي غاية الجلاء، بحيث لا جلاء فوقه، فيتحير العقل، ويمتنع عن تمام إدراكه كالنور إذا اشتد يمتنع البصر عن الرؤية .
ثم إنهم بعد الاتفاق على عدم وقوع تصوره بكنهه اختلفوا، فمنهم من اقتصر على ذلك ، وجوز إمكانه ، وهو يلوح من كلام الشيخ الرئيس ، وقال عليه الرحمة بالاستحالة، وهو مذهب قدماء الفلاسفة والصوفية
Introduction of the book. His existence is not outside [the mind] so that it could be divided. Among His sanctifications is His saying: 'And it is not conceived' – if read with a damma as a sign of the impossibility of the future, then its meaning is that Allah the Exalted is not perceived in His essence, because it is impossible for Him to occur in the mind, and He has no likeness such that He could occur. And even if it were assumed, it would not be a source of disclosure necessarily, because that which is separate cannot be a source of disclosure for another separate thing. As for the impossibility of His occurrence in the mind, there are aspects: The first: if He occurred in the mind, then His occurrence would either be with His remaining outside, so there must be something common between them that is necessitated by the other, so the distinguisher which is the individuation would not be identical to [the essence]. For one of them has a reality and a distinguisher; also, multiplicity of the particular would be necessitated, or with its annulment outside, which is even more impossible. Here it also appears that the particular as an individual does not occur in the mind, nor in the sense when perceiving. The second: that the referent of external existence is identical to His essence, without any additional aspect, so if it occurred in the mind, it would be a referent for both external and mental accidents simultaneously. The third: that His essence is the referent for the predicate 'necessary', without any additional aspect, so if it occurred in the mind, it would be necessary and in need of the mind which is its locus. And here there are two sublime proofs for the impossibility of conceiving His essence: The first: what was expressed by the Greatest Shaykh, the Supreme Imam, the support of saints and the pious, the mine of guidance, the seal of the bezel of sainthood, a good deed from the good deeds of the Master of Messengers who was a saint while Adam was between water and clay, Shaykh Muhyi al-Milla wa al-Din Muhammad ibn Arabi (may his secret be sanctified and Allah be pleased with him) in 'al-Futuhat al-Makkiyya': that He, the Exalted, is contrary to created things, there is no correspondence between Him and His creation at all, and how could He resemble the one who accepts an image with the one who accepts an image? Knowledge of Allah the Exalted is too noble for the perception of intellect and soul, except that it is obligatory – He is Exalted and Sanctified – and everything that is uttered concerning created things, or imagined concerning composites and other things, then Allah the Exalted in the view of sound intellect is contrary to that; imagination cannot apply to Him. End of summary. This is solid speech, which only he who comes to Allah with a sound heart can understand. The second: what is transmitted from the First Teacher: that He, glorified, is so extremely manifest that there is no manifestation above it, so the intellect is bewildered and prevented from fully perceiving Him, like light when it becomes intense, the eye is prevented from seeing. Then, after agreeing on the non-occurrence of conceiving His essence, they differed: some restricted themselves to that and deemed it possible, which appears from the speech of al-Shaykh al-Ra'is, and he [i.e., another] said – may mercy be upon him – [that it is] impossible, and that is the doctrine of the ancient philosophers and Sufis.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and theological discussion within a commentary on logic, addressing the question of whether Allah's essence (kunh) can be conceptualized (tasawwur). The author begins by establishing a principle: Allah's existence is not 'outside' in a way that would allow mental representation. He then presents three arguments for the impossibility of Allah's essence occurring in the mind: first, that it would entail a shared element between mental and external existence, undermining individuation; second, that since His external existence is identical to His essence without extra aspect, mental occurrence would make Him subject to both external and mental accidents; third, that His essence is the referent of 'necessary existence', so mental occurrence would entail both necessity and dependence on the mind. The author then introduces two 'sublime proofs' (burhanani 'arshiyyan). The first is from Ibn Arabi, emphasizing absolute dissimilarity between Creator and creation; no image or concept can capture Him. The second is from Aristotle (the 'First Teacher'): God is so manifest that the intellect is dazzled, like light too bright for vision. Finally, the author notes that despite agreement that actual conceptualization does not occur, there is disagreement on whether it is possible in principle. Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) seems to allow its possibility, while others (ancient philosophers and Sufis) deem it impossible. This passage thus distinguishes between actual impossibility and potential impossibility, and guards against the misunderstanding that 'not conceptualized' means 'not known at all' – rather, it is a recognition of divine transcendence.

Key Terms
الكنه
التصور
الجزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why would it be impossible for Allah's essence to occur in the mind while remaining outside?
A) Because the mind is too weak to grasp anything divine.
B) Because there would need to be a common element between the mental and external instances, which would undermine the uniqueness of Allah's essence.
C) Because Allah does not want to be known.
D) Because the external existence would be annihilated.

Key Takeaway
Allah's essence cannot be conceptualized because it would entail logical absurdities such as multiplicity, subjection to accidents, and dependence, all contradicting His absolute oneness and necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Apply this to the example of a created thing (e.g., a tree) to show the difference.
