Two Proofs and a Disagreement on Divine Incomprehensibility
Two proofs from Ibn Arabi and Aristotle for the incomprehensibility of Allah, with the subsequent scholarly disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the proofs of Ibn Arabi and Aristotle for the impossibility of fully perceiving Allah's essence and identify the subsequent scholarly disagreement on whether conceptualization is absolutely impossible or only de facto impossible.

Core Idea
Ibn Arabi's absolute dissimilarity and Aristotle's blinding light both show that Allah cannot be fully known by creation.

After establishing logical impossibility, the author presents two 'sublime proofs' from major authorities: the greatest Sufi master, Ibn Arabi, and the First Teacher, Aristotle. These proofs underscore divine transcendence from both a mystical and a rational perspective. The passage concludes with a crucial scholarly dispute: is conceptualization of Allah's essence impossible in principle or just does not occur? This distinction has deep implications for theology and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two proofs
The author introduces 'two sublime proofs' (برهانان عرشيان). The first is from Ibn Arabi in al-Futuhat al-Makkiyya, emphasizing absolute dissimilarity. The second is from Aristotle (al-mu'allim al-awwal), using the analogy of intense light. Identify the beginning of each proof in the text.
2
Summarize Ibn Arabi's proof
Ibn Arabi argues that Allah is completely unlike creation; there is no correspondence whatsoever. Therefore, no concept or image can capture Him. The intellect and soul are incapable of knowing His essence. Note the key phrase: 'يخالف المخلوقات، لا مناسبة بينه وبين خلقه البتة'.
This proof is from a mystical perspective, emphasizing God's transcendence beyond all categories.
3
Summarize Aristotle's proof
Aristotle's proof uses an analogy: just as intense light blinds the eye, Allah's extreme manifestation overwhelms the intellect, preventing full perception. This shows that the obstacle is not obscurity but excessive clarity.
Key phrase: 'جلي غاية الجلاء، بحيث لا جلاء فوقه، فيتحير العقل'
4
Identify the disagreement
After agreeing that actual conceptualization does not occur, scholars differ on whether it is possible in principle. Some (like Ibn Sina) deem it possible but non-actual; others (ancient philosophers and Sufis) deem it absolutely impossible.
The text says: 'فمنهم من اقتصر على ذلك وجوز إمكانه، وهو يلوح من كلام الشيخ الرئيس، وقال عليه الرحمة بالاستحالة'
5
Evaluate the implications
Consider how the two proofs lead to different conclusions about possibility. Ibn Arabi's absolute dissimilarity suggests impossibility; Aristotle's analogy might allow for some kind of indirect knowledge. Reflect on why this disagreement matters for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الكتاب وجوده إلا أنه ليس خارجا ، حتى يكون مقسما .
ومن جملة تقديساته قوله: (ولا يتصور)، إن قُرِئَ بِضَمَ عَلَامَةِ بيان استحالة الاِسْتِقْبَالِ ، فمعناه لا يدرك الله تعالى بكنهه ؛ لامتناع حصوله في الذهن ، ولا شبح له حتى يحصل، ولو فرض لا يكون مبدأ الانكشاف بالضرورة؛ لأن المباين لا يكون مبدأ للانكشاف لمباين آخر .
وأما امتناع حصوله في الذهن فمن وجوه، الأولن أنه لو حصل في الذهن فحصوله إما مع بقائه في الخارج، فلا بد من أمر مشترك بينهما واجب عن الآخر، فلا يكون المميز الذي هو التعين عينه.
لأحدهما حقيقة ، ومميز وأيضا يلزم تكثر الجزئي أو مع بطلانه في الخارج، وهو أشد استحالة ههنا ظهر أيضا أن الجزئي من حيث هو شخص لا يحصل في الذهن، ولا الحاسة حين الإدراك .
الثاني أن مصداق الوجود الخارجي عين ذاته تعالى، من غير حيثية زائدة ، فلو حصل في الذهن لكان مصداقا للعوارض الخارجية والذهنية معا . الثالث: أن ذاته مصداق حمل الواجب ، بلا حيثية زائدة، فلو حصل في الذهن لكان واجبا ومحتاجا إلى الذهن الذي هو محله.
وههنا برهانان عرشيان على امتناع تصوره بكنهه، الأول: ما أفاده الشيخ الأكبر، والإمام الأعظم، سند الأولياء والأتقياء ، معدن الهداية خاتم فص الولاية، حسنة من حسنات سيد المرسلين الذي كان وليا وآدم بين الماء والطين، الشيخ محيي الملة والدين محمد بن العربي قدس سره، ورضي الله عنه في (الفتوحات المكية): أنه تعالى يخالف المخلوقات، لا مناسبة بينه وبين خلقه البتة ، وكيف لا يشبه من يقبل المثال بمن يقبل المثال ، فالعلم بالله تعالى عزيز عن إدراك العقل والنفس، إلا أنه واجب تعالى وتقدس، وكلما يتلفظ به في حق المخلوقات، أو يتوهم في المركبات وغيرها فالله تعالى في نظر العقل السليم بخلاف ذلك ، لا يجوز عليه الوهم انتهى ملخصا هذا كلام متين ، يعجز عن فهمه إلا من أتى الله بقلب سليم.
الثاني ما نقل عن المعلم الأول : أنه سبحانه جلي غاية الجلاء، بحيث لا جلاء فوقه، فيتحير العقل، ويمتنع عن تمام إدراكه كالنور إذا اشتد يمتنع البصر عن الرؤية .
ثم إنهم بعد الاتفاق على عدم وقوع تصوره بكنهه اختلفوا، فمنهم من اقتصر على ذلك ، وجوز إمكانه ، وهو يلوح من كلام الشيخ الرئيس ، وقال عليه الرحمة بالاستحالة، وهو مذهب قدماء الفلاسفة والصوفية
Introduction of the book. His existence is not outside [the mind] so that it could be divided. Among His sanctifications is His saying: 'And it is not conceived' – if read with a damma as a sign of the impossibility of the future, then its meaning is that Allah the Exalted is not perceived in His essence, because it is impossible for Him to occur in the mind, and He has no likeness such that He could occur. And even if it were assumed, it would not be a source of disclosure necessarily, because that which is separate cannot be a source of disclosure for another separate thing. As for the impossibility of His occurrence in the mind, there are aspects: The first: if He occurred in the mind, then His occurrence would either be with His remaining outside, so there must be something common between them that is necessitated by the other, so the distinguisher which is the individuation would not be identical to [the essence]. For one of them has a reality and a distinguisher; also, multiplicity of the particular would be necessitated, or with its annulment outside, which is even more impossible. Here it also appears that the particular as an individual does not occur in the mind, nor in the sense when perceiving. The second: that the referent of external existence is identical to His essence, without any additional aspect, so if it occurred in the mind, it would be a referent for both external and mental accidents simultaneously. The third: that His essence is the referent for the predicate 'necessary', without any additional aspect, so if it occurred in the mind, it would be necessary and in need of the mind which is its locus. And here there are two sublime proofs for the impossibility of conceiving His essence: The first: what was expressed by the Greatest Shaykh, the Supreme Imam, the support of saints and the pious, the mine of guidance, the seal of the bezel of sainthood, a good deed from the good deeds of the Master of Messengers who was a saint while Adam was between water and clay, Shaykh Muhyi al-Milla wa al-Din Muhammad ibn Arabi (may his secret be sanctified and Allah be pleased with him) in 'al-Futuhat al-Makkiyya': that He, the Exalted, is contrary to created things, there is no correspondence between Him and His creation at all, and how could He resemble the one who accepts an image with the one who accepts an image? Knowledge of Allah the Exalted is too noble for the perception of intellect and soul, except that it is obligatory – He is Exalted and Sanctified – and everything that is uttered concerning created things, or imagined concerning composites and other things, then Allah the Exalted in the view of sound intellect is contrary to that; imagination cannot apply to Him. End of summary. This is solid speech, which only he who comes to Allah with a sound heart can understand. The second: what is transmitted from the First Teacher: that He, glorified, is so extremely manifest that there is no manifestation above it, so the intellect is bewildered and prevented from fully perceiving Him, like light when it becomes intense, the eye is prevented from seeing. Then, after agreeing on the non-occurrence of conceiving His essence, they differed: some restricted themselves to that and deemed it possible, which appears from the speech of al-Shaykh al-Ra'is, and he [i.e., another] said – may mercy be upon him – [that it is] impossible, and that is the doctrine of the ancient philosophers and Sufis.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and theological discussion within a commentary on logic, addressing the question of whether Allah's essence (kunh) can be conceptualized (tasawwur). The author begins by establishing a principle: Allah's existence is not 'outside' in a way that would allow mental representation. He then presents three arguments for the impossibility of Allah's essence occurring in the mind: first, that it would entail a shared element between mental and external existence, undermining individuation; second, that since His external existence is identical to His essence without extra aspect, mental occurrence would make Him subject to both external and mental accidents; third, that His essence is the referent of 'necessary existence', so mental occurrence would entail both necessity and dependence on the mind. The author then introduces two 'sublime proofs' (burhanani 'arshiyyan). The first is from Ibn Arabi, emphasizing absolute dissimilarity between Creator and creation; no image or concept can capture Him. The second is from Aristotle (the 'First Teacher'): God is so manifest that the intellect is dazzled, like light too bright for vision. Finally, the author notes that despite agreement that actual conceptualization does not occur, there is disagreement on whether it is possible in principle. Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) seems to allow its possibility, while others (ancient philosophers and Sufis) deem it impossible. This passage thus distinguishes between actual impossibility and potential impossibility, and guards against the misunderstanding that 'not conceptualized' means 'not known at all' – rather, it is a recognition of divine transcendence.

الشيخ الرئيس
الفتوحات المكية

Disagreement on the Possibility of Conceptualizing God's Essence
Author outlines scholarly differences on whether conceptualization of God's essence is possible after agreeing it does not occur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main positions on the conceptual possibility of God's essence and explain the epistemological distinction between acquired and presential knowledge as they relate to this debate.

Core Idea
The core debate is not about whether God can be known to exist, but whether His essence can be conceptually grasped.

In this passage, the author presents a nuanced debate among theologians and philosophers regarding the human capacity to form a concept (tasawwur) of God's essence. While all agree that such conceptualization has not occurred in this life, they diverge on whether it is even possible in principle. This lesson will guide you through the different positions and the crucial epistemological distinction that underlies one side of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared premise
All parties agree that full conceptualization of God's essence has not occurred in reality. Locate the phrase 'after they agreed on the non-occurrence' to confirm this consensus.
2
Locate the point of disagreement
Despite the consensus on non-occurrence, scholars differ on possibility. Some, including Ibn Sina, permit its possibility in principle. Others, like ancient philosophers, Sufis, and Imam Abu Hanifa, assert absolute impossibility. Identify these two camps in the text.
3
Extract the author's position
The author explicitly sides with the impossibility view, stating that 'the mentioned evidences indicate its impossibility in itself, and that is correct.' Note the phrase 'imtina'uhu bi al-dhat' as the key term.
4
Analyze the linguistic note
The final sentence offers an alternative reading: if the verb is read with a fatha, it means God does not perceive things by acquired knowledge (idrak husuli). This shifts the debate from human conceptualization to the nature of divine knowledge, implying presential knowledge (huduri).
This linguistic nuance reinforces the impossibility of a conceptual grasp of God's essence.
5
Connect to epistemology
Reflect on how the distinction between acquired (husuli) and presential (huduri) knowledge is crucial in Islamic epistemology. Here, the impossibility of conceptualizing God's essence is linked to the idea that divine knowledge is not conceptual but immediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم بعد الاتفاق على عدم وقوع تصوره بكنهه اختلفوا، فمنهم من اقتصر على ذلك، وجوز إمكانه، وهو يلوح من كلام الشيخ الرئيس، وقال عليه الرحمة بالاستحالة، وهو مذهب قدماء الفلاسفة والصوفية، كثّرهم الله تعالى، وهكذا نقل عن إمامنا الأعظم، إمام الأئمة، الباذل جهده في إعلاء السنة وقمع البدعة الإمام أبي حنيفة رحمة الله الكوفي، والدلائل المذكورة تعطي امتناعه بالذات، وهو الصواب. وإن قُرِئ بفتح علامة الاستقبال فمعناه أنه لا يدرك الأشياء إدراكا حصوليا.
Then after they agreed on the non-occurrence of conceptualizing His essence (bi-kunhihi), they differed. Among them are those who limited themselves to that and permitted its possibility, which appears in the words of the Chief Teacher (Ibn Sina), and some said impossibility, which is the doctrine of the ancient philosophers and Sufis – may Allah multiply them. And likewise it is transmitted from our greatest Imam, the Imam of Imams, who exerted his effort in elevating the Sunna and suppressing innovation, Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) of Kufa: the mentioned evidences indicate its impossibility in itself, and that is correct. And if it is read with a fatha on the sign of the future tense, then its meaning is that He does not perceive things by way of acquired knowledge (idrak husuli).
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage discusses the theological and philosophical debate on whether the human mind can ever form a conceptualization (tasawwur) of God's essence. While all agree that such full knowledge has not occurred, some, including the Chief Teacher Ibn Sina, hold that it is possible in principle. Others, including the ancient philosophers and Sufis, maintain absolute impossibility. The author cites a quote from Imam Abu Hanifa supporting the impossibility view. The passage ends with a linguistic note: if the word 'la yudrik' is read with a fatha on the letter of istiqbal (the ي), it means God does not perceive things in an acquired manner (husuli), implying His knowledge is presential. This lesson highlights the key epistemological position that God's essence is beyond conceptual grasp.

Key Terms
تصوره بكنهه (tasawwuruhu bi-kunhihi): Conceptualization of His essence as it really is (in itself). [Note: This phrase is standard in Islamic philosophy; kunh means inner reality.]
الشيخ الرئيس (al-shaykh al-ra'is): Ibn Sina (Avicenna), the leading philosopher. [Note: Stable title for Ibn Sina.]
إدراك حصولي (idrak husuli): Acquired knowledge, as opposed to presential knowledge (huduri). It denotes conceptual representation. [Note: In later Islamic philosophy, husuli is contrasted with huduri; this note anticipates that distinction.]

The Self-Evidence of 'Ilm: Dispute and Defense
Author presents dispute on 'ilm's self-evidence and defends it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the arguments for and against the self-evidence of the concept of 'ilm and evaluate the author's position.

Core Idea
Even the most basic concept in Islamic epistemology is contested; the author argues 'ilm is self-evident, like light.

This passage presents a foundational debate in Islamic epistemology: is the concept of knowledge ('ilm) self-evident (badihi) or does it require theoretical reasoning (nazari)? The author adopts the first position, comparing 'ilm to light and pleasure. A marginal commentary then sharpens the discussion by raising objections and offering a defense. Understanding this dispute is crucial for appreciating how Islamic scholars establish the starting points of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Positions
Locate in the Arabic text the three views on the concept of 'ilm: badihi, nazari, and impossible. Note which one the author chooses.
The phrase 'وَاخْتَلَفُوا فِي تَصَوُّرِ الْعِلْمِ' introduces the divergence.
2
Extract the Author's Argument and Analogy
Find the author's statement that 'ilm is 'among the most self-evident things' and his comparison to light and pleasure. Understand why analogy is used.
The author uses تشبيه (analogy) to strengthen his claim.
3
Analyze the Objection from the Commentary
In the marginal commentary, identify the objection: that self-evidence of a specific (e.g., knowledge of light) does not necessarily entail self-evidence of the general (absolute 'ilm). The objection requires two conditions: the general being essential (dhati) to the specific, and the specific known in essence (bi al-kenh).
The objection is introduced with 'وَيُرَدُّ عَلَيْهِ الْمَنْعَانِ الْمَشْهُورَانِ'.
4
Examine the Author's Response
Find the counter-argument using the example of Zayd known necessarily as a writer: if Zayd is known by necessity with respect to writing, then the concept of writing is self-evident by implication. Similarly, knowledge of light is a distinguished instance that necessitates the self-evidence of absolute 'ilm.
The response begins with 'وَيُمْكِنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ'.
5
Evaluate the Argument's Strength
Assess whether the response successfully rebuts the objection. Consider the author's concession that 'refining its reality is difficult' and think about whether the analogy from Zayd to 'ilm is valid.
The conclusion 'نَعَمْ، تَنْقِيحُ حَقِيقَتِهِ عَسِيرٌ' shows humility about fully grasping 'ilm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم: أنهم اختلفوا في تصور العلم، فذهب جمع إلى أنه وجمع إلى أنه نظري ممكن التصورِ ، وجمع إلى أنه مأيوس، والمصنف اختار الأول، وقال: (وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ) ، أي العلم مِنْ أَجْلَى الْبَدِيهِيَّاتِ )، فإذن لا حاجة إلى المعرف إلا لفظا، ولذا استعمل فيه لفظ المدرك، (كَالنُّور وَالسُّرُور) ، أي كما أنهما بديهيان كذلك العلم، أو كالعلم بالنور والسرور . قال في «الحاشية»: «فيه إشارة إلى ما هو المشهور، من أن بديهة الخاص يستلزم بديهة العام ويرد عليه المنعان المشهوران: من أن ذلك إنما يتم لو كان العام ذاتيا للخاص، ويكون الخاص متصورا بالكنه، وكلاهما ممنوعان. ويمكن أن يقال: إن زيدا إذا كان معلوما بالضرورة، من حيث الكتابة، سواء كان معلوما بالكنه أو بالوجه كان بالضرورة مستلزما لبديهة معلوم الكتابة، فنقول العلم بالنور معلوم ممتاز من حيث العلم، الذي من لوازمه الامتياز عن الجهل به ، نعم، تنقیح حقيقته عسير ، وفيه ما فيه .
Know: They differed concerning the concept of knowledge. Some held that it (is badihi), some held that it is theoretical (nazari) and can be conceived, and some held that it is impossible. The author chose the first and said: 'The truth is that it'—i.e., knowledge—'is among the most self-evident things.' Therefore, there is no need for a definition except verbally. Hence, he used the term 'perceiver'. (Like light and pleasure), i.e., just as they are self-evident, so is knowledge; or like knowledge of light and pleasure. He said in the marginal commentary: 'There is an indication in this of what is well-known: that the self-evidence of the specific entails the self-evidence of the general. And it is met by the two well-known objections: that this is only valid if the general is essential (dhati) to the specific, and the specific is conceived in its essence (bi al-kenh); both are denied. And it can be said: if Zayd is known by necessity with respect to writing, whether he is known by essence or by aspect, then necessarily it entails the self-evidence of the known writing. So we say: knowledge of light is known and distinguished in terms of knowledge, from whose necessary concomitants is distinction from ignorance of it. Yes, refining its reality is difficult, and there is much debate.'
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on whether the concept of 'ilm (knowledge) is self-evident (badihi) or requires theoretical reasoning (nazari). The author presents three positions: (1) it is badihi, (2) it is nazari and can be conceived, (3) it is impossible to conceive. The author adopts the first position, stating that knowledge is among the most self-evident things, like light and pleasure. Therefore, it does not need a definition except for verbal clarification. He then brings in a marginal commentary that discusses the objection that if a specific concept (e.g., knowledge of light) is self-evident, it does not necessarily entail that the general concept (e.g., absolute knowledge) is self-evident. The objection states that this inference only holds if the general is essential (dhati) to the specific and the specific is conceived in its essence, both of which are denied. The author then offers a possible response: if a particular instance (like Zayd being known as a writer) is known by necessity, then it necessarily entails the self-evidence of the property of writing. Similarly, knowledge of light is a distinguished instance that necessarily implies the self-evidence of the absolute concept of knowledge. He concludes that, although refining the true nature of 'ilm is difficult, the above argument holds.

Distinguishing Conception (Tasawwur) from Assent (Tasdiq)
Defines the division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) based on the presence or absence of belief in a propositional relation.

Core Idea
Knowledge splits into two: bare conception and judgmental assent.

This passage from the foundational text on logic defines the fundamental division of knowledge. Understanding this division is essential because it determines how we approach every subsequent topic in logic and across the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage divides knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Note that this is a basic binary classification.
2
Find the distinguishing criterion
Assent involves belief (i'tiqad) in a propositional relation (nisba khabariyya). Conception is cognition without such belief.
The relation can be positive or negative, categorical or conditional.
3
Analyze the types of assent
Assent is further subdivided into certainty (jazm) and opinion (zann). Both are genuine acts of assent, differing only in strength.
This is contrary to Imam al-Razi's view that assent requires multiple components.
4
Understand the disagreement and resolution
Imam al-Razi claimed assent is a combination of three perceptions and a judgment. The author, via al-Sayyid, affirms the simpler definition as correct.
Al-Sayyid asserts that understanding the purpose of logic confirms the philosophers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَقْسِيمُ الْعِلْم إلَى تَصَوُّر وتصديق
فَإِنْ كَانَ اعْتِقَادًا لِنِسْبَة خَبَريَّة، إيجابية كانت أو سلبية، حملية كانت أو اتصالية أو انفصالية فَتَصْدِيقٌ وحُكُمْ، وينقسم إلى الجزم والظن، وهما نوعان تحته، خلافا للإمام رَحِمَهُ اللهُ حيث زعم أنه عبارة عن مجموع الإدراكات الثلاثة والحكم. قال السيد قدس سره الشريف: إن من علم غرض هذا الفن أيقن أن الحكماء على الحق، والله أعلم بالصواب.
وَإِلا، وإن لم يكن اعتقادا فَتَصَورٌ سَاذج، وهو إحساس وتخيل
The division of knowledge into conception and assent. For if it is a belief in a propositional relation, whether positive or negative, whether categorical or conditional or disjunctive, then it is assent and judgment; and it is divided into certainty and opinion, both being types under it, contrary to Imam (al-Razi), may Allah have mercy on him, since he claimed that it is an expression of the sum of three perceptions and judgment. Al-Sayyid, may his secret be sanctified, said: Whoever knows the purpose of this art is certain that the philosophers are upon the truth, and Allah knows best what is correct. Otherwise, if it is not a belief, then it is simple conception, which is sensation and imagination.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage defines the division of knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). The author explains that if a cognition involves belief (i'tiqad) in a propositional relation (nisba khabariyya)—whether affirmative or negative, categorical or conditional—then it is called assent and judgment. Assent is then divided into certainty (jazm) and opinion (zann), which are its two types, contrary to Imam al-Razi who held that assent is a composite of three perceptions plus judgment. The author quotes al-Sayyid that comprehending the goal of this discipline confirms that the philosophers are correct. If the cognition does not involve belief in a relation, then it is simple conception (tasawwur sadhaj), which is sensation and imagination.

C) Assent is only about categorical propositions.
D) Conception is about external objects; assent is about mental forms.

Key Takeaway
Assent is distinguished from bare conception by the presence of belief in a propositional relation.

The Critique of al-Razi's View on Tasdiq as Act
Author critiques al-Razi's and later scholars' views on whether tasdiq is an act or perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key positions on the nature of tasdiq and articulate the author's critique against al-Razi and later scholars.

Core Idea
Tasdiq is perception, not act — and confusing them leads to epistemological error.

In this passage, the author firmly rejects the claim that assent (tasdiq) is an act (fi'l), as argued by Imam al-Razi. Instead, he affirms the well-known position that tasdiq is a type of perception (idrak), just like conception (tasawwur). He also dismisses views that tasdiq is merely an adjunct to perception, and criticizes later scholars for reducing the difference between tasdiq and tasawwur to a mere relation of objects. Understanding this clarifies the nature of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the view being critiqued
Locate al-Razi's claim that tasdiq is an act (fi'l). This is the primary target of the author's criticism.
The author states: 'not as al-Imam claimed that it is an act'.
2
Find the author's counter-assertion
The author asserts that tasdiq is a perception (idrak), aligning with the well-known position. Note the contrast between fi'l and idrak.
The passage says: 'this statement is explicit that assent is perception'.
3
Analyze the rejection of a weaker view
Some might argue tasdiq is an adjunct to perception (lahiq al-idrak), like doubt or illusion. The author dismisses this without elaboration.
The phrase 'and it may be said' indicates a weak objection that is rejected.
4
Critique of later scholars' position
Later scholars claimed the difference between tasawwur and tasdiq is only in their relation to objects (bi-i'tibar al-muta'allaq), not in essence. The author calls this 'foolish' and promises further refutation.
The author uses 'sakhaafatuhu bayyinah' (its foolishness is evident).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم لا حجر في التصور فيتعلق بكل شيء حتى يتعلق بالتصديق ثم هذا الكلام نص على أن التصديق إدراك كما هو المشهور لا كما زعم الإمام أنه فعل كما لا يخفى وربما يقال إنه من لواحق الإدراك وليس بإدراك كالشك والوهم وزعم المتأخرون أن التغاير بينهما إنما هو باعتبار المتعلق لا في حقيقتهما وسخافته بينة كما سيظهر فيما بعد إن شاء الله تعالى
Yes, there is no restriction in conception, so it relates to everything, even to assent. Then this statement is explicit that assent is perception, as is well-known, not as al-Imam claimed that it is an act, as is not hidden. And it may be said that it is an adjunct of perception, not a perception, like doubt and illusion. The later scholars claimed that the difference between them is only in terms of the relation, not in their essence, and its foolishness is evident, as will appear later, God willing.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary correcting wrong opinions about the nature of tasdiq. It begins by affirming that tasawwur has no restriction and can relate to anything, even to tasdiq itself. Then it states that the previous discussion (in Lesson 1) clearly indicates that tasdiq is a perception (idrak), which is the well-known position. It rejects Imam al-Razi's claim that tasdiq is an act (fi'l), not a perception. Some might argue that it is a mere adjunct to perception, like doubt or illusion, which the author dismisses. The author then criticizes later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun) who claimed that the difference between tasawwur and tasdiq is only in their relation to objects, not in their essence, deeming this view obviously foolish. The passage foreshadows further clarification later. An Islamic example: the assent that 'prayer is obligatory' is itself a mental perception of a truth, not an external action like raising the hands.

Tasawwur as a Relative Type
Defining tasawwur as a relative type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of singular perception and explain why tasawwur is a relative type (naw' idafi) rather than a real genus.

Core Idea
Tasawwur is a relative genus, not a real one.

This lesson unpacks the first part of the passage, where the author lists the types of perception that relate to singular concepts and clarifies that tasawwur is a relative type (naw' idafi) encompassing seven subtypes. The author warns against the mistake of treating tasawwur as a real genus (jins haqiqi), a common error among those who neglect the philosophers' usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of singular perception
Locate the terms: wahm, ta'aqqul, and two others (the text says 'these four relate to the singular'). They are subtypes under tasawwur.
The passage also lists types for propositional perception: wahm, takhayyul, shakk.
2
Recognize tasawwur as a relative type
The author explicitly states 'tasawwur is a relative type under which are seven subtypes' (فالتصور نوع إضافي تحته أنواع سبعة). Note the term 'idafi' (relative) as opposed to 'haqiqi' (real).
3
Refute the misconception of a real genus
The author criticizes those who think tasawwur is a real genus, saying they neglect the philosophers' position. Understand that a real genus would have its own essence, while a relative type is defined by its relation to its subtypes.
This distinction is crucial for later arguments about the nature of definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتوهم وتعقل، وهذه الأربعة متعلقة بالمفرد ووهم وتخيل وشك، وهذه الثلاثة متعلقة بالخبر والقضية، فالتصور نوع إضافي تحته أنواع سبعة، ومن زعم التصور نوعا حقيقيا فقد غفل عما عليه الفلاسفة. (وَهُمَا نَوْعَانِ مُتَبَايِنَانِ مِنَ الْإِدْرَاكِ ضَرُورَة))، والاستدلال باختلاف لوازمها مبني على كون اللوازم لوازم الماهية ، وللمنع فيه مجال . ثم هذا الكلام نص على أن التصديق إدراك، كما هو المشهور، لا كما زعم الإمام أنه فعل (۲) ، كما لا يخفى وربما يقال: إنه من لواحق الإدراك ، وليس بإدراك ، كالشك والوهم . وزعم المتأخرون أن التغاير بينهما إنما هو باعتبار المتعلق ، لا في حقيقتهما، وسخافته بينةٌ ، كما سيظهر فيما بعد إن شاء الله تعالى.
And wahm and ta'aqqul – these four relate to the singular; and wahm, takhayyul, and shakk – these three relate to the report and the proposition. So tasawwur is a relative type under which are seven subtypes. Whoever thinks tasawwur is a real type has neglected what the philosophers are upon. (And they are two mutually distinct types of perception necessarily), and the inference by difference of their concomitants is based on the concomitants being concomitants of the essence, and there is room for denial. Then this statement is textual evidence that tasdiq is perception, as is well-known, not as the Imam claimed that it is an action, as is not hidden. And perhaps it is said: It is among the adjuncts of perception and not perception, like doubt and illusion. The later scholars claimed that the difference between them is only with respect to the relatum, not in their reality, and its absurdity is clear, as will appear later, God willing.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of tasawwur and tasdiq. It begins by listing types of perception related to singular concepts (wahm, ta'aqqul, etc.) and those related to propositions (wahm, takhayyul, shakk), establishing that tasawwur is a relative type with seven subtypes. The author then emphasizes that tasawwur and tasdiq are two essentially distinct types of perception, rejecting the later scholars' view that they differ only by relation. The argument uses the difference in their concomitants (lawazim) to infer the distinction. The author also rejects the view of Imam al-Ghazali (and others) that tasdiq is an action rather than perception, insisting it is perception as commonly held. The absurdity of the later view is noted as clear.

نوع إضافي

D) A single indivisible perception

Key Takeaway
Tasawwur is a relative type, not a real genus, and its subtypes include specific kinds of singular perception.

Tasdiq as Perception and Its Distinction from Tasawwur
Defining tasdiq as perception and refuting alternative views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that tasdiq is an action (fi'l) and explain why tasawwur and tasdiq are essentially distinct types of perception, not merely different by relation.

Core Idea
Tasdiq is perception, not action; it differs from tasawwur in essence.

In this lesson, we analyze the second part of the passage, where the author affirms that tasdiq is a type of perception (idrak), contrary to Imam al-Ghazali's view that it is an action. The author also dismisses the later scholars' opinion that the difference between tasawwur and tasdiq is only relative to their objects, insisting that they are two essentially distinct types of perception. The argument uses the difference in their concomitants (lawazim) to support this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim about tasdiq
The author states that tasdiq is perception (idrak), as is well-known, and rejects the claim that it is an action (fi'l). Note the reference to 'the Imam' (al-Ghazali) who held the action view.
2
Examine the argument for essential distinction
The author points to the difference in concomitants (lawazim) between tasawwur and tasdiq as evidence that they are different in reality. However, he also notes that this inference depends on the concomitants being essential, which might be contested.
The phrase 'وللمنع فيه مجال' indicates the argument is not conclusive on its own.
3
Refute the later scholars' view
The author dismisses the later scholars' claim that the difference between tasawwur and tasdiq is only with respect to their object (muta'allaq). He calls it absurd (sakhafa), promising further elucidation later.
This sets up a deeper discussion on the essential nature of these two cognitive acts.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Resolving the Famous Doubts on the Unity of Knowledge and the Known
Author presents and resolves two doubts about the unity of knowledge and the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the two dialectical objections against the unity of conceptual knowledge and its object, and explain the author's responses using logical and epistemological tools.

Core Idea
Conceiving the essence of assent may be impossible, akin to conceiving God.

The claim that knowledge and the known are identical in conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) seems to lead to contradictions when we consider that conception relates to everything, including assent. The author of Bahr al-Ulum examines two famous doubts and offers nuanced responses. In this lesson, we will walk through these objections and the logical moves used to defend the original position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim being defended
The principle that knowledge and known are identical in essence in conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) and distinct in assent (tasdiq). Recognize that this is the foundational assertion under attack.
This claim is a common epistemological stance in Islamic logic.
2
Analyze the first doubt
If conception relates to everything, including assent, then conceiving assent would make knowledge and known one, contradicting the claim that they are different in reality. Extract the logical structure of this objection.
The doubt exploits the apparent universality of conception.
3
Evaluate the first response and its refinement
The initial answer (unity specific to conceptual knowledge) is rejected as ad hoc. The stronger answer from the gloss: conceiving the essence of assent may be impossible, like conceiving God. Assess why this is more robust.
This answer avoids special pleading by invoking impossibility.
4
Analyze the second doubt
Conception of a substance or quantity would make the mental form fall under both substance/quantity and quality (knowledge), leading to a single thing under two categories. Identify the categorical problem.
This doubt challenges the categorical classification of knowledge.
5
Examine the proposed resolutions
The author rejects the answer that 'quality' can be a common accident. Instead, he endorses al-Dawwani's metaphorical application of 'quality' to knowledge. Understand why metaphor is preferred.
Metaphor allows maintaining the distinctness of categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نَعَمْ، لَا حَجْرَ فِي التَّصَوُّرِ، فَيَتَعَلَّقُ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ، حتى يتعلق بالتصديق وبنقيضه، (وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مشهور ، وهو: أن العلم والمعلوم متحدان بالذات فاذا المترتب على تَصَوَّرْنَا التَّصْدِيقَ ،  َأي المُصَدَّقَ به ؛ بناء على أنه يتعلق بكل شيء، فهو متحد القول بأن ) معه بالتصديق ، فَهُمَا وَاحد ، وقد قُلْتُمْ إِنَّهُما متخالفان حقيقة ) | التصور يتعلق بكل شيئ حتى والجواب بأن اتحاد العلم والمعلوم مختص بالعلم التصوري، دون التصديق التصديقي، فتخصيص للقواعد العقلية، فلا يسمع، مع أنه لو قرر الاعتراض بأنا إذا تصورنا التصديق بالكنه لزم اتحادهما، فلا يتوجه. قال المصنف في الحاشية» مجيبا عن هذا التقرير: إن القدر المسلم تعلق التصور بكل شي ولو بالوجه، ومن الجائز أن يكون تصور التصديق بالكنه من المستحيلات. كتصور الباري عز اسمه، وهو ليس بشيء؛ فإن منع إمكان تصور الماهية الإمكانية من المكابرات ، وخلاف مصرحات الشيخ . وههنا إشكال آخر قريب منه ، وهو : أنا إذا تصورنا الحقيقة الجوهرية أو الكمية يلزم اتحادهما دهما مع الحقيقة العلمية، التي هي من مقولة «الكيف»، فيلزم اندراج شيء واحد تحت مقولتين، فلا يتجه الجواب بأن الكيف ربما يطلق على الشيء الموجود في الموضوع الغير القابل للقسمة والنسبة بالذات، وهو عرض عام للجوهر وغيره، واتحاد الجوهر معه غير محال . وربما يطلق على ماهية من شأن وجودها في الأعيان ذلك، وصدقه بهذا المعنى على العلم ممنوع، على أن ذلك لا يتجه لو قرر السؤال في الإضافة. نعم، لو أجيب بارتكاب التجوز في إطلاق مقولة «الكيف على العلم، كما ارتكب المحقق الدواني ، سواء كان الكيف مشتركا لفظيا أو لا، فله وجه، فتأمل فيه.
Yes, there is no restriction in conception (tasawwur), so it relates to everything, even to assent (tasdiq) and its opposite. And here there is a famous doubt: that knowledge and the known are identical in essence. So if it follows from our conception of assent, i.e., the object of assent, based on the fact that it relates to everything, then it is identical with assent, so they are one, and you have said they are different in reality. The answer is that the unity of knowledge and known is specific to conceptual knowledge, not to assenting knowledge, but a specification of rational principles is not accepted. Moreover, if the objection were formulated as: if we conceive assent in its essence, then they would be one, then it does not apply. The author said in the gloss answering this formulation: The established point is that conception relates to everything, even if in some aspect, and it is possible that conceiving assent in its essence is impossible, like conceiving the Exalted God, Who is not a thing. For denying the possibility of conceiving a contingent essence is stubbornness and contrary to the explicit statements of the Shaykh. And here is another doubt close to it: that if we conceive the substantial reality or quantity, it would necessitate their union with the reality of knowledge, which is from the category of quality, so a single thing would fall under two categories. The answer that quality may be applied to a thing existing in a subject not divisible by essence, and it is a common accident to substance and others, and the union of substance with it is not impossible, does not apply because quality in this sense is denied of knowledge. And perhaps it is applied to an essence whose mode of existence in external things is that, and its truth in this sense for knowledge is denied. Moreover, that does not apply if the question is formulated concerning relation. Yes, if answered by resorting to metaphorical usage in applying the category of quality to knowledge, as al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani did, whether quality is a homonym or not, then it has a face; so ponder it.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents two epistemological doubts concerning the relationship between knowledge (especially conception) and the known. The first doubt: If conception relates to everything, including assent, then conceiving assent would make knowledge and known identical, contradicting their distinctness. The answer is that the principle that knowledge and known are one is specific to conceptual knowledge, but this is rejected as ad hoc. A stronger answer from the author is that conceiving the essence of assent may be impossible, akin to conceiving God. The second doubt: If we conceive a substance or quantity, the mental form would unite with the reality of knowledge which is a quality, causing one thing to fall under two categories. The author examines possible answers: applying 'quality' as a broad common accident or as a specific essence, but finds them insufficient. He finally endorses al-Dawwani's approach of metaphorical application of the category 'quality' to knowledge. The lesson underscores the complexity of defending the distinctness of tasawwur and tasdiq against such dialectical objections.

Objection to the Division of Knowledge: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author states the division of knowledge and presents a famous objection about the unity of knowledge and known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical objection against the division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq and explain the key response using the doctrine of ittihad al-'ilm wa al-ma'lum.

Core Idea
The objection that knowledge and known are one challenges the very distinction between concept and assent.

The division of knowledge into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) is foundational in Islamic logic. However, this division faces a famous objection grounded in the philosophical thesis that knowledge and its object are identical in essence. In this lesson, we will analyze the objection and the sophisticated response that invokes the limits of conception. Understanding this debate sharpens your grasp of how logicians defend basic classifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division of knowledge
The passage opens by stating the division: knowledge is divided into tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). This is the starting point for the objection.
Tasawwur is simple apprehension; tasdiq is judgment.
2
Grasp the objection based on ittihad al-'ilm wa al-ma'lum
The objection argues that since knowledge and the known are one (ittihad), if we conceive assent (tasdiq), the object of that conception is assent itself. Hence, conception and assent would be identical, contradicting their supposed difference.
The phrase 'fadha shakk mashhur' introduces this well-known doubt.
3
Analyze the first response and its rejection
One answer claims that the unity of knowledge and known only applies to conceptual knowledge, not to judgmental knowledge. This is dismissed as an arbitrary specification (takhsis) that violates rational principles.
The rejection uses the phrase 'fala yusma' – it is not accepted.
4
Understand the refined response from the author's gloss
A more refined answer: conception pertains to everything in some aspect, but it may be impossible to conceive the essence (al-kunh) of assent itself, just as it is impossible to conceive the essence of Allah. Thus, the objection does not arise because the object of conception is not the real essence.
This response appeals to the distinction between conceiving something in a limited aspect versus its true essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم العلم إلى تصور وتصديق. الإشكال: وبنيقيضه، وههنا شك مشهور، وهو: أن العلم والمعلوم متحدان بالذات، فإذا المترتب على تصورنا التصديق أي المصدق به، بناء على أنه يتعلق بكل شيء، فهو متحد معه بالتصديق، فهما واحد، وقد قلتم إنهما متخالفان حقيقة. والجواب: بأن اتحاد العلم والمعلوم مختص بالعلم التصوري دون التصديق التصديقي، فتخصيص للقواعد العقلية فلا يسمع، مع أنه لو قرر الاعتراض بأنا إذا تصورنا التصديق بالكنه لزم اتحادهما فلا يتوجه. قال المصنف في الحاشية مجيبا عن هذا التقرير: إن القدر المسلم تعلق التصور بكل شيء ولو بالوجه، ومن الجائز أن يكون تصور التصديق بالكنه من المستحيلات كتصور الباري عز اسمه، وهو ليس بشيء؛ فإن منع إمكان تصور الماهية الإمكانية من المكابرات، وخلاف مصرحات الشيخ.
Division of knowledge into concept and assent. The objection: And by its opposite, and here is a famous doubt, which is: that knowledge and the known are identical in essence, so what follows from our conception of assent, i.e., the object of assent, on the basis that it pertains to everything, then it is identical with assent, so they are one, and you have said that they are different in reality. The answer: that the union of knowledge and the known is specific to conceptual knowledge, not to assentive assent, so it is a specification of rational rules and is not accepted, moreover if the objection were formulated that when we conceive assent in its essence, their union would follow, then it is not directed. The author said in the gloss responding to this formulation: The accepted amount is that conception pertains to everything even in some aspect, and it is possible that conceiving assent in its essence is among the impossibilities, like conceiving the Exalted Creator, and He is not a thing; for denying the possibility of conceiving a possible essence is among the acts of obstinacy and contrary to the explicit statements of the Shaykh.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the division of knowledge into tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). It then presents a famous objection against this division based on the unity of knowledge and the known. The objection argues that since knowledge and the known are identical in essence, if we have a conception of assent, the object of that conception is assent itself, making them one, which contradicts the claim that concept and assent are different. The first answer given is that the identity of knowledge and known is specific to conceptual knowledge, not to judgmental knowledge, but this is criticized as an arbitrary specification violating rational principles. Then the author (al-Dawani) provides a more refined answer: conception pertains to everything in some respect, but it may be impossible to conceive the essence of assent itself, similar to the impossibility of conceiving the essence of Allah; thus, the objection does not hold. This highlights the subtlety of the relationship between knowledge and its object in the Islamic logical tradition.

Knowledge as a Single Reality and the Unknown Essence Objection
Argues knowledge is one reality and refutes the unknown essence objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that knowledge is a single reality and evaluate the response to the objection from unknown essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge's unity is logically necessary for classifying conception and assent as its species.

In this passage, the author defends the unity of knowledge (haqiqa wahida) against the objection that if we cannot know its essence, how can we assert its unity? He uses a logical argument and an analogy with humanity. You will learn how even an unknown essence can still be a common genus, and how this reasoning supports the classification of knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq).

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states that knowledge is a single reality (haqiqa wahida) shared by all particular knowledges via semantic commonality (ishtirak ma'nawi). Find this assertion in the Arabic.
This claim is crucial for the classification of knowledge into types.
2
Extract the supporting reason
If knowledge were not a single reality, it would be impossible for tasawwur and tasdiq to be species (naw') under it. A genus must be one for its species to share it.
3
Understand the objection
The objector (mu'tarid) asks: if the essence (kunh) of knowledge is unknown, how can you claim it has a single reality? Locate the phrase 'فإن قلت'.
4
Analyze the response
The author replies that even if the essence is unknown, it is impossible for different instances of knowledge to have no essential commonality. He analogizes: humans differ individually but share the same reality of humanity.
This shows that unknown essence does not negate logical unity.
5
Evaluate the conclusion
The author concludes that denying essential commonality would break any generic unity between individuals. Thus, the unity of knowledge is necessary for coherent classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نعلم قطعا أن العلم حقيقة واحدة مشتركة بين العلوم الخاصة اشتراكا معنويا، وإن كان هذا عسى أن يكون مكابرة. وأيضا لو لم يكن حقيقة واحدة لما صح جعل التصور والتصديق نوعين تحته. فإن قلت: من يقول بمجهولية حقيقة العلم كيف يقنع بهذا؟ قلت: اختلاف العلوم بحيث لا يكون ذاتي مشترك بينها ضروري الاستحالة، وإن كانت مجهولة الكنه، كما أن اختلاف الأناسي بالحقيقة ضروري الاستحالة، وإن كان كنهها مجهولا، وإبداء مثل هذه الاحتمالات يبطل اتحاد نوعي بين كل شخصين. وعلى هذين الجوابين يلزم أن يكون العلم دعوى بالجوهر شيئا، وبالكم شيئا آخر، وهكذا هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Then we know with certainty that knowledge is a single reality common among the particular knowledges with a semantic commonality, although this may be obstinacy. Also, if it were not a single reality, it would not be correct to make conception and assent two types under it. If you say: One who says that the reality of knowledge is unknown, how is he satisfied with this? I say: The difference of knowledges such that there is no essential commonality between them is necessarily impossible, even if their essence is unknown, just as the difference of humans in reality is necessarily impossible, even if their essence is unknown. And raising such possibilities invalidates the generic unity between every two individuals. And on these two answers, it follows that knowledge is a claim by substance something, and by quantity something else, and so on this, and Allah knows best the truth.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage provides a positive argument that knowledge must be a single reality. The author states that we know with certainty that knowledge is one common reality shared by all particular knowledges by semantic commonality (i.e., not merely homonymy). If it were not one reality, then it would be incorrect to place tasawwur and tasdiq as two species under a single genus of knowledge. A potential objection is raised: if the essence of knowledge is unknown (as some schools claim), how can we assert its unity? The author responds that even if the essence is unknown, the difference in realities without any essential commonality is necessarily impossible. He gives an analogy: humans differ individually, but it is impossible that their reality differs essentially; similarly, knowledges must share an essential nature. Presenting possibilities of non-commonality would negate any generic unity between individuals. Thus, the unity of knowledge is established even if its full essence is not known.

اشتراك معنوي

Key Takeaway

Knowledge as Scientific Form: Solving the Union Problem
Author presents his unique solution to the union problem by defining knowledge as the scientific form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the scientific form resolves the union of knowledge and the known, and identify the author's critique of the alternative view.

Core Idea
The scientific form is both knowledge and known under different aspects, resolving the union without collapse.

In this passage, the author presents his unique solution to a longstanding philosophical problem: how can knowledge and the known be united? He defines knowledge as the scientific form (al-surah al-’ilmiyya), which plays a dual role. This lesson will walk you through his analysis and show you how it refines earlier positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize that the 'union' (ittihad) issue concerns whether knowledge and the known are identical or separate. The passage addresses this by proposing a refined definition.
The union problem is a key issue in Islamic epistemology, especially in falsafah and kalam.
2
Extract the author's solution
The author states that 'knowledge in the issue of union means the scientific form'. Note the two aspects: from the aspect of occurrence in the mind, it is known; from the aspect of subsistence and mental accidents, it is knowledge.
The phrase 'from the aspect' (min haythu) is crucial for understanding the dual role.
3
Contrast with the rejected view
Some claim that knowledge is the sum of the known and accidents. The author rejects this because accidents have no role in disclosure (inkishaf) and would lead to composition from two categories.
The rejection is based on the function of knowledge: to disclose, which accidents cannot do.
4
Understand the refinement
The scientific form is a single entity with dual consideration. This resolves the union problem without making knowledge and known identical or entirely separate.
This refinement avoids the extremes of pure identity or pure separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحله على ما تفردت به، مطابقا لما قال بعض الأجلة، غير شاعر بها: أن العلم في مسألة الاتحاد أي اتحاد العلم والمعلوم بمعنى الصورة العلمية، فإنها من حيث الحصول في الذهن معلوم، والأظهر أن يقال من حيث هي معلوم، ومن حيث القيام به والاكتناف بالعوارض الذهنية علم، لا كما زعم البعض أن العلم مجموع المعلوم والعوارض، ويلتزم عدم الاتحاد أصلا، كيف والعوارض لا دخل لها في الانكشاف، وأيضا يلزم التركيب من المقولتين.
And its solution, according to what I have uniquely (adopted), conforming to what some of the greats said, unaware of it: that knowledge in the issue of union, i.e., the union of knowledge and the known, means the scientific form. For it, from the aspect of its occurrence in the mind, is known, and the more apparent is to say from the aspect that it is known, and from the aspect of its subsistence in it and its being encompassed by mental accidents, it is knowledge, not as some claimed that knowledge is the sum of the known and the accidents, necessitating non-union at all. How can that be when accidents have no role in disclosure? Also, it would require composition from two categories.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the author's own unique solution to the problems raised earlier about the union of knowledge and the known. He defines knowledge in this context (mas'alat al-ittihad) as the scientific form (al-surah al-ilmiyya). This form, when considered from the aspect of its occurrence in the mind, is the 'known' (ma'lum); but when considered from the aspect of its subsistence in the mind and its being accompanied by mental accidents (such as being a mental existence), it is 'knowledge' ('ilm). This resolves the identity issue without collapsing the two. The author rejects the view that knowledge is the sum of the known and accidents, because accidents do not contribute to disclosure and would lead to composition from two categories. Thus, the scientific form serves a dual role without becoming two separate things.

Key Takeaway

Knowledge as a Perceptual State and its Division
Author refines definition of knowledge and divides it into tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the mental form and the perceptual state, and identify the division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not the mental form itself but a unitive perceptual state that attaches to it.

This passage refines the definition of knowledge by clarifying that the mental form is not knowledge in itself. Instead, knowledge is a perceptual state that mixes unitively with the form, like the concept of 'writer' with 'human'. This sets the stage for the division of knowledge into simple conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), which are essentially distinct but can relate to the same object.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the mental form from the perceptual state
Notice that the author says the form becomes knowledge only through a perceptual state that mixes with it. This is not a physical mixture but a conceptual one.
2
Understand the unitive mixture
The mixture is likened to 'writer' with 'human'. The perceptual state is abstracted from the form and united in existence.
3
Identify the division into tasawwur and tasdiq
The perceptual state divides into simple conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). The form is called these only accidentally.
4
Analyze the analogies
The difference between tasawwur and tasdiq is like sleep and wakefulness to the same person, or like speed to motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم العلم إلى تصور وتصديق (ثُمَّ بَعْدَ التَّفْتِيشِ يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ تِلْكَ الصُّورَةَ) ليست علما في ذاتها ؛ لأن العلم حقيقة واحدة، والصورة ليست كذلك ، بل (إِنَّمَا صَارَتْ) تلك الصورة ( عِلْمًا ؛ لِأَنَّ الْحَالَةَ الْإِدْرَاكِيَّةَ قَدْ خَالَطَتْ بِوُجُودِهَا الاِنْطِبَاعِيَّ)، أي بوجودها المنطبع الذهن، وليس خلطا انضماميا حتى يلزم كون الصورة عالمة، أو كقيام عرضين بموضوع؛ حتى يلزم ما يلزم على شارح «التجريد»، بل(۱) (خَلْطَا رَابِطِيَّا اتِّحَادِيَّا ، كخلط الكاتب بالإنسان، وتلك منتزعة من الصورة متحدة معها في الوجود، قائمة بالذهن ؛ فإن أحد المتحدين إذا قام بالموضوع وجب قيام المتحد الآخر بالضرورة ، كما صرح به الشيخ في «قاطيغورياس الشفاء». فإن قلت: فكيف يكون العلم كيفا ؟ . :قلت كونها انتزاعية لا ينافي الكيفية، كالكيفيات التي في الكميات . ثم إن تلك الحالة ليست لازمة للصورة، ولا ذاتية لها، وإلا لما وجدت في الخارج معراة عنها، بل من الأعراض التي لا تعرض إلا في الذهن أو الحاسة، وهي تنقسم إلى أنواعها من الذوق والشم وغيرهما، كما أشار إليه، (كَالْحَالَةِ الذَّوْقِيَّةِ)، أي الحالة الإدراكية التي في القوة الذائقة (بِالْمَذُوقَاتِ)، أي الكيفية المذوقة، وهي أنواع الطعم حين حصولها في الذائقة، (فَصَارَتْ صُورَةً ذَوْقِيَّةٌ ، وَالسَّمْعِيَّةُ بِالْمَسْمُوعَاتِ)، وهي الأصوات حين حصولها في السامعة ، (وَهَكَذَا) ، كالشمية بالمشمومات ، وهي الروائح، والتوهم بالوهميات حين حصولها في التوهم، والتخيل بالجزئيات الحاصلة في الحس المشترك ، والتعقل بالكليات الحاصلة في العقل. (وَتِلْكَ الْحَالَةُ تَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى التَّصَوُّرِ وَالتَّصْدِيقِ)، الذي هو الإذعان بالذات، والصورة إنما يقال لها التصور والتصديق بالعرض ، (فَتَفَاوُتُهُمَا كَتَفَاوُتِ النَّوْمِ وَالْيَقْظَةِ الْعَارِضَتَيْنِ لِذَاتِ وَاحِدَةٍ ، الْمُتَبَايِنَتَيْنِ بِحَسَبِ حَقِيقَتِهِمَا)؛ إذ لا يلزم من اتحاد المعروض اتحاد العوارض، فاندفع التقرير الأول، وتفاوت التصور المتعلق بالتصديق كتفاوت السرعة العارضة للحركة، فاندفع التقرير الثاني. وأيضا العارض من مقولة «الكيف»، والمعروض من أي مقولة كانت، فاندفع الإشكال الأخير.
Division of knowledge into concept and assent. (Then after investigation it is known that that form) is not knowledge in itself, because knowledge is a single reality, and the form is not like that. Rather, (that form only becomes knowledge because the perceptual state has mixed with its imprinting existence), i.e., with its existence imprinted in the mind, and it is not a juxtappositional mixture such that the form would be knowing, nor like the subsistence of two accidents in one subject, such that what follows for the commentator of al-Tajrid would follow. Rather, (it is a relational, unitive mixture, like the mixture of writer with human. And that state is abstracted from the form, united with it in existence, subsisting in the mind. For when one of the two united things subsists in a subject, the subsistence of the other united thing is necessarily required, as the Shaykh stated in Qatighuriyas of al-Shifa. If you say: How then is knowledge a quality? I say: Its being abstractive does not negate quality, like the qualities that are in quantities. Moreover, that state is not necessary for the form, nor essential to it, otherwise it would not have existed in the external world stripped of it. Rather, it is among the accidents that only occur in the mind or the sense, and it divides into its types such as taste, smell, etc., as he indicated, (like the gustatory state), i.e., the perceptual state that is in the tasting faculty (regarding tasted things), i.e., the tasted quality, which are the types of taste when they occur in the taste faculty, (so it becomes a gustatory form, and the auditory state regarding audible things), i.e., sounds when they occur in the hearing faculty, (and so on), like the olfactory regarding smelled things, i.e., odors, and the estimative regarding estimable things when they occur in the estimation, and the imaginative regarding the particulars occurring in the common sense, and the intellectual regarding the universals occurring in the intellect. (And that state divides into concept and assent), which is affirmation essentially, and the form is only called concept and assent accidentally. (So their difference is like the difference of sleep and wakefulness that occur to a single essence, which are disparate with respect to their reality), because the unity of the subject does not necessitate the unity of the accidents. Thus the first claim is refuted. And the difference of the concept related to the assent is like the difference of speed occurring to motion. Thus the second claim is refuted. Also, the accident is from the category of quality, and the subject is from whatever category, thus the last objection is refuted.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This is a definitional commentary passage that refines the concept of knowledge and its primary division into tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). The author, building on earlier discussions, clarifies that the mental form (sura) is not knowledge in itself; knowledge is a perceptual state (hala idrakiyya) that mixes unitively with the form in the mind, similar to how 'writer' mixes with 'human'—not as a separate accident but as an abstracted attribute that subsists through the form. This addresses the objection that if knowledge were a quality (kayf), then the form would have to be knowing, which is absurd. The author responds by noting that this state is an abstractive quality, akin to qualities found in quantities (e.g., the dimensions of a body), so it does not make the form itself knowing. He then enumerates various perceptual states: gustatory, auditory, olfactory, estimative, imaginative, and intellectual, each corresponding to the sense or faculty involved. Importantly, this state divides into tasawwur and tasdiq: tasawwur is the simple conception of something without judgment, while tasdiq is the assent (idhan) to a proposition. The author emphasizes that the form itself is called tasawwur or tasdiq only accidentally, through its association with this state. To explain the difference between tasawwur and tasdiq, he uses the analogy of sleep and wakefulness: both occur to the same person (subject) but are distinct in reality. Similarly, both tasawwur and tasdiq can relate to the same object but are essentially different. He also compares the difference of the concept related to the same assent to the difference of speed in motion—an accidental variation. Finally, he notes that the accident (the state) is from the category of quality, while the subject (the mental content) can be from any category, thereby resolving the earlier objection that knowledge cannot be a quality if its subject is a form from another category.

C) It is identical to the form itself.
D) It is an external entity that enters the mind.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is a unitive perceptual state that attaches to the mental form, dividing into simple conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), which are essentially distinct.

The Logical Opposition Between Necessary and Acquired Knowledge
Author examines logical opposition types between necessary and acquired knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of opposition between two epistemological concepts and explain why necessary and acquired knowledge are not true contradictories.

Core Idea
Necessary and acquired knowledge are not contradictories; they relate as privation and possession.

In Islamic logic, knowledge is often divided into necessary (badīhī) and acquired (kasbī). But are they true opposites? In this passage, the author examines the logical relationship between them, ruling out several types of opposition before concluding that they are like privation and possession. This lesson walks you through that analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms under discussion
The passage discusses 'al-badīha' (intuitive knowledge) and 'al-kasbiyya' (acquired knowledge). Note that they are opposites, but what kind?
These are not the only categories of knowledge; presential and divine knowledge are also mentioned.
2
List the four types of opposition in logic
Recall the standard types: tanāquḍ (contradictories), taḍādd (contraries), taḍāyuf (correlatives), and 'adam wa malaka (privation and possession). The author tests each.
Contradictories cannot both be false; contraries cannot both be true but can both be false; correlatives imply each other; privation/possession involves a subject capable of both states.
3
Eliminate each type based on conditions
Contradictories: if necessary and acquired were contradictories, every subject would have to be one or the other, but concrete objects are neither. Nor are they contraries (since contraries require potential succession) or correlatives (obvious).
The author argues that only privation and possession fits because the subject (human knowledge) can potentially change from one to the other.
4
Conclude the opposition type
Thus, necessary and acquired knowledge are opposites in the mode of privation and possession. This means that necessary knowledge can, in principle, become acquired, which has implications for divine knowledge.
This conclusion prepares for the next lesson on restricting the division of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليعلم أن الكسبية التوقف على النظر، والبديهة تقابلها، وليس بينهما التقابل بين تقابل الإيجاب والسلب؛ لأن المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسلب لا يخلو موضوع ما عنهما، وإلا لزم ارتفاع النقيضين، والموجودات العينية [كلها خالية عنهما. ولا التضاد أو التضايف، وهو ظاهر، بل إنما يتصور التضاد أو العدم والملكة. ولا بد في التضاد من إمكان تعاقبها على موضوع واحد، فكل ما يتصف بأحد الضدين أمكن اتصافه بالآخر، ولا بد في المتقابلين بالعدم والملكة من إمكان اتصاف موضوع العدم بالملكة، وعلى التقديرين فلا بد من إمكان اتصاف الضروري بالكسبية، ومن البين أن الحضوري يمتنع اتصافه بالكسبية، وكذا القديم [من العلم]؛ إذ لو أمكن كونه كسبيا لأمكن حصوله بعد الحركة الفكرية، فلا يكون قديما، هذا خلف. فإذن لا بد من تخصيص الْمَقْسِم بالتصور والتصديق الحادثين.
Let it be known that acquired knowledge depends on investigation, and intuition is its opposite, but between them there is not the opposition of affirmation and negation; because the two opposites by affirmation and negation do not leave any subject devoid of both, otherwise the two contradictories would be raised, and concrete existents are all devoid of both. Nor is it the opposition of contrariety or correlation, which is obvious. Rather, only contrariety or privation and possession can be conceived. And for contrariety, it is necessary that they can succeed each other on a single subject, so everything that is characterized by one of the contraries can be characterized by the other. And for the two opposites by privation and possession, it is necessary that the subject of privation can be characterized by possession. And on both suppositions, it is necessary that necessary [knowledge] can be characterized by acquired [knowledge]. And it is evident that presential knowledge cannot be characterized by acquired knowledge, and likewise ancient [knowledge of God]; for if it could be acquired, it could be obtained after intellectual motion, so it would not be ancient—this is absurd. Therefore, it is necessary to restrict the divided [i.e., the division of knowledge] to the temporal tasawwur and tasdiq.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage examines the relationship between necessary (badīhī) and acquired (kasbī) knowledge. The author argues that they are not true contradictories (like existence and non-existence), because if they were, every existent would have to be either necessary or acquired. But many things—like concrete objects—are neither knowledge nor the objects of knowledge. Nor are they contraries (like black and white) or correlatives (like father and son). Rather, they are like privation and possession (like blindness and sight). For both contraries and privation/possession, it is possible for the subject to acquire the opposite: a healthy person can become blind, and a blind person can be cured. Similarly, necessary knowledge could in principle become acquired—but this is impossible for presential knowledge (knowledge by presence) and for God's eternal knowledge, because if they could be acquired, they would be subject to change and would not be eternal. Hence, the division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq must be restricted to temporal (created) knowledge, excluding God's knowledge. This is a key refinement in Islamic logic: the categories of necessary and acquired apply only to human knowledge, not to divine omniscience.

Key Terms

البديهة
التقابل
التضاد
العدم والملكة

A) Contradictories (tanāquḍ)
B) Contraries (taḍādd)
C) Correlatives (taḍāyuf)
D) Privation and possession ('adam wa malaka)

Key Takeaway
Necessary and acquired knowledge are opposites in the mode of privation and possession, not strict contradictories.

Why Divine Knowledge is Excluded from the Division of Knowledge
Author restricts division of knowledge to temporal human knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq applies only to temporal (ḥādith) knowledge, and not to God's eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is eternal and presential, so it cannot be acquired through reflection.

The author argues that since necessary knowledge can in principle become acquired (like privation turning into possession), this would be impossible for God's eternal knowledge. Therefore, the division of knowledge into concept and assent must be restricted to human temporal knowledge. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Note the conclusion: division only for temporal knowledge
The passage ends by stating that the divided (al-maqsīm) must be restricted to temporal concept and assent (al-tasawwur wa al-tasdiq al-ḥādithayn). This is the key takeaway.
Temporal here means created, contingent, subject to time.
2
Follow the reasoning: why divine knowledge cannot be acquired
If necessary knowledge can become acquired (as argued from the opposition type), then God's knowledge would be changeable, which is absurd. The author uses the term 'presential knowledge' (al-ḥuḍūrī) to describe direct knowledge without representation.
Presential knowledge is a key concept in Suhrawardi's Illuminationist philosophy, but here it simply means knowledge by direct presence, not mediated.
3
Identify the key terms: presential and ancient
The passage mentions 'al-ḥuḍūrī' (presential) and 'al-qadīm' (ancient, i.e., eternal) as types of knowledge that cannot be acquired. These exclude divine and angelic knowledge from the human division.
The argument is that if such knowledge could be acquired, it would not be eternal. This protects divine immutability.

Source Text

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Divine Knowledge and the Tasawwur/Tasdiq Distinction
Author refutes the claim that divine knowledge is not tasawwur or tasdiq, distinguishing between heresy and terminological convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between heretical and terminological interpretations of the claim that God's knowledge is not tasawwur or tasdiq, and identify the logical error in denying divine assent.

Core Idea
Denying that God is a 'musaddiq' is heresy; merely restricting the terms is harmless.

In this passage, the author addresses a controversial claim about divine knowledge. Some have argued that the logical division into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) does not apply to God. But as the author shows, this claim can be understood in two very different ways: one that is heretical and one that is merely terminological. Understanding the difference is crucial for protecting the Islamic doctrine of divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible meanings of the claim
Read the Arabic carefully: 'إن أريد أنه تعالى ليس مصدقا للصوادق' and 'وإن أريد مجرد الاصطلاح'. The author presents two interpretations: one about God's actual relationship to truth, and one about the applicability of technical terms.
2
Assess the first interpretation
If the claim means that God does not assent to true propositions (is not a musaddiq), why is that heretical? It contradicts the Qur'anic affirmation that God knows all things and judges truth.
The author labels it 'kufr sarih'.
3
Assess the second interpretation
If the claim means only that we do not apply the terms 'tasawwur' and 'tasdiq' to God in technical discourse due to lack of scientific purpose, what does the author say? He says there is no dispute (la mushahata fihi).
This is a permissible terminological choice.
4
Evaluate the conclusion
Even if we accept the terminological restriction, does it follow that God's knowledge lacks these attributes? The author says 'لا يلزم منه أن لا يكون العلم القديم تصورا وتصديقا' – it does not entail that ancient knowledge is not tasawwur and tasdiq. The restriction is about our discourse, not God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قيل: إن علم الواجب سبحانه لا يكون تصورا وتصديقا، إن أريد أنه تعالى ليس مصدقا للصوادق، كما يلوح من الأسفار الأربعة، فهو باطل، بل هو كفر صريح. وإن أريد مجرد الاصطلاح لعدم تعلق الغرض العلمي بالعلم القديم فلا مشاحة فيه، لكن لا يلزم منه أن لا يكون العلم القديم تصورا وتصديقا.
And what is said: that the knowledge of the Necessary [Existent], glorified be He, is not tasawwur and tasdiq—if it is meant that He, exalted, is not an assenter to true propositions, as is intimated from the Four Journeys, then it is false, rather it is explicit disbelief. And if it is meant a mere terminological restriction due to the lack of scientific purpose regarding ancient knowledge, then there is no dispute about it, though it does not entail that ancient knowledge is not tasawwur and tasdiq.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that God's knowledge is not tasawwur (conception) or tasdiq (assent). The author draws a sharp distinction: if one means that God is not an 'assenter' (muṣaddiq) to true propositions—implying that God does not know or believe truths—that is false and heretical, as it contradicts the divine omniscience affirmed in revelation and philosophy. However, if one merely intends a terminological restriction—that the formal division of knowledge into conception and assent is only applied to human knowledge because it serves no purpose in discussing God's eternal knowledge—then there is no substantive disagreement (lā mushāḥa fīhi). But even in that case, one cannot conclude that God's knowledge lacks the attributes of conception and assent. The passage is a critical refinement that prevents the division from being misused to deny divine attributes.
