Knowing by Aspect: The Mirror Argument
Author discusses and defends the possibility of knowing by aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that knowledge by aspect (wajh) is possible and distinguish it from knowledge by essence (kunnah).

Core Idea
Knowing by aspect is possible because the aspect's essence acts as a mirror for the thing.

Can we know a thing without knowing its essence? The scholars discuss 'knowing by aspect' (wajh). Some argue that if we cannot know the essence, we cannot know the aspect either, but the author presents a counterargument: the aspect itself can be known by its essence, serving as a mirror. This lesson unpacks that analogy and the critique that follows.

Guided Path
1
Define the two types of knowledge
Knowledge by essence (kunnah) is knowing the inner reality of a thing. Knowledge by aspect (wajh) is knowing it through one of its attributes. Understand this distinction.
2
Extract the objection to knowing by aspect
Find the claim: 'الوجه في علم الشيء بالوجه لا يتصور بالكنه ولا بالوجه'. This asserts that the aspect cannot be conceived by essence or by aspect, implying impossibility.
This is the position the author will critique.
3
Identify the response using the mirror analogy
Look for 'بل الوجه في علم الشيء بالوجه يحصل كنهه بحيث يكون مرآة لملاحظة ذي الوجه'. The aspect's own essence is obtained and serves as a mirror for the thing having the aspect.
This shows that knowing the aspect is not circular; it is a distinct cognition.
4
Analyze the author's critique of the impossibility
The author says the impossibility of knowing by essence is accepted, but not by aspect. He gives two reasons: first, the argument doesn't rest on its premises; second, knowledge of essence is only for necessary conceptions, so it cannot apply if all are theoretical.
He also notes that what is essential to a thing may be accidental to its aspect, and vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كنها لنفسه، فلم يحصل تصور . وتممه بعض الأجلة من المتأخرين، وقال: إذا لم يحصل تصور لم يحصل تصديق ؛ لأن التصديق متوقف على التصور. ولا يخفى أن هذا إنما يتم لو فرض كسبية جميع التصورات أيضا ، أما لو فرض كسبية التصديقات بأسرها مع ضرورية بعض التصورات فلا يتم ذلك. قال بعض من الأجلة: إن الوجه في علم الشيء بالوجه لا يتصور بالكنه ولا بالوجه وإلا لكان المقصود بالعرض مقصودا بالذات والمتصور بالذات متصورا بالعرض في ملاحظة واحدة، بل الوجه في علم الشيء بالوجه يحصل كنهه بحيث يكون مرآة لملاحظة ذي الوجه ، فحينئذ يجوز أن يحصل كنه الوجه في أزمنة غير متناهية من مباديه. وبالجملة ما ذكر في امتناع التصور بالكنه مسلم، وفي امتناع التصور بالوجه غير مسلم، ولا يخفى ما فيه من الاختلال، أما أولا : فلأنه لا على شيء من مقدماته ، اللهم إلا أن يكون معارضة ، وأما ثانيا فلأن العلم بكنه الشيء مختص بالضروريات عنده، فلا يمكن على تقدير نظرية الكل. نعم، يرد عليه أن يكون مبادئ الكنه والوجه مشتركة ؛ فإن ما هو ذاتي لشيء عرضي لوجهه، وما هو ذاتي لوجهه يجوز أن يكون عرضيا لذلك الشيء، والله أعلم.
It [the thing] is not known by its essence, so no conception occurs. And some of the later eminent scholars completed it, saying: If no conception occurs, no assent occurs, because assent is dependent on conception. It is not hidden that this only holds if it is assumed that all conceptions are also acquired; but if it is assumed that all assents are acquired while some conceptions are necessary, then it does not hold. Some of the eminent scholars said: Indeed, the aspect in knowledge of a thing by the aspect is not conceived by the essence nor by the aspect; otherwise, the intended accidentally would be intended essentially, and that which is conceived essentially would be conceived accidentally in one observation. Rather, the aspect in knowledge of a thing by the aspect is such that its essence is obtained as a mirror for observing the thing having the aspect. Then it is permissible that the essence of the aspect be obtained in infinite times from its principles. In summary, what was mentioned regarding the impossibility of conception by essence is accepted, while the impossibility of conception by aspect is not accepted. Yet the disturbance in it is not hidden. First, because it does not rely on any of its premises, unless it is a counterargument. Second, because knowledge of the essence of a thing is specific to necessary [conceptions] according to him, so it is impossible given the supposition that all [conceptions] are theoretical. Yes, it is objected that the principles of the essence and the aspect are common; for what is essential to a thing is accidental to its aspect, and what is essential to its aspect may be accidental to that thing. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a previous statement that something is not known by its essence, so no conception of it occurs. The author first presents a completion by later scholars: they argue that if no conception occurs, then no assent occurs, because assent depends on conception. However, the author notes that this only holds if all conceptions are assumed to be acquired (kasbi). If some conceptions are necessary (daruri), then the dependency does not necessarily apply. Then another scholar is quoted: the 'aspect' (wajh) in knowing a thing by its aspect is not itself conceived by essence or by aspect; otherwise it would lead to confusion between essential and accidental intention. Rather, the aspect serves as a mirror through which the thing is observed, and its essence may be obtained in infinite time from its principles. The author then critiques: the impossibility of conception by essence is accepted, but the impossibility by aspect is not accepted, for two reasons: first, the argument against it does not stand on its own premises, unless it is a counterargument; second, the knowledge of the essence of a thing, according to that scholar, is restricted to necessary conceptions, so it cannot hold if all are theoretical. Furthermore, the principles of essence and aspect are shared, and what is essential to one may be accidental to the other. The passage ends with a humble 'And God knows best.'

Why Conceptualization Cannot Come from Assent
Author refutes objection of mutual derivation between tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why, according to the Islamic logical tradition, conceptualization (tasawwur) cannot be derived from assent (tasdiq) based on the predicate requirement.

Core Idea
A definiens must be a predicate, but a proposition (assent) is not a predicate.

In the study of Islamic logic, a key distinction is made between conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Some have argued that one can be derived from the other, avoiding circularity. The author of this passage responds by showing why such derivation is impossible: definitions require predicates, and assents are not predicates. Understanding this helps clarify the foundations of knowledge acquisition in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Notice the hypothetical objector's claim: all conceptualizations could be theoretical, and some assents are necessary, so conceptualization might be acquired from assent (or vice versa) without circularity or infinite regress.
This objection challenges the standard view that concepts must come from concepts and assents from assents.
2
Understand the Author's Response
The author firmly denies this possibility: 'And conceptualization is not known from assent nor vice versa.' Recognize that this is the conclusion the author aims to prove.
The response is categorical, with separate arguments for each direction.
3
Analyze the Key Premise
The author's argument for the first part (no tasawwur from tasdiq) hinges on the nature of definition: 'the definiens is predicated of the definiendum.' Grasp that a proper definition (whether essential or accidental) must be a predicate—i.e., it can be said of the thing defined.
Predication here means the definiens can be attached to the definiendum in a proposition like 'Man is a rational animal.'
4
Recognize Why Assent Fails
Assent (tasdiq) is a proposition that affirms or denies something; it is not itself a predicate. Since the definiens must be a predicate, and assent is not one, assent cannot serve as a definiens. Therefore, conceptualization cannot be derived from assent.
The author notes that the minor premise (that definiens must be a predicate) is open to denial, but if one accepts it, the conclusion follows.
5
Reflect on the Additional Nuance
The author also addresses a potential counter: that definiens gives only quiddity (kuna) or conceptualization by aspect. He points out that even allowing this would permit deriving quiddity from something entirely different, which is unacceptable. This reinforces the original denial.
The phrase 'so ponder' invites the reader to think through the implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان لقائل أن يقول (۱) : يجوز أن يكون جميع التصورات نظرية، وبعض التصديقات ضرورية، أو بالعكس، ويكتسب التصور من التصديق، أو بالعكس، فلا دور ولا تسلسل أجاب بقوله: (وَلَا يُعْلَمُ التَّصَوُّرُ مِنَ التَّصْدِيقِ وَبِالْعَكْس)، فالأول لأَنَّ الْمُعَرِّفَ مَقُولٌ على المعرف، والتصديق ليس مقولا، والصغرى في حيز المنع ، والاستدلال بأن المعرف لا يفيد إلا الكنه أو التصور بالوجه، والأول بالذاتيات والثاني بالعرضيات، وكلاهما مقولان غير تام؛ فإن من يجوز اكتساب التصور من التصديق يجوز حصول الكنه من المباين أيضا، فتفكر ، والله أعلم بالصواب (۲) .
And since there is one who might say: It is permissible that all conceptualizations are theoretical, and some assents are necessary, or vice versa, and conceptualization is acquired from assent, or vice versa, so there is no circularity nor regress. He answered by his saying: 'And conceptualization is not known from assent nor vice versa.' The first (i.e., that conceptualization is not known from assent) is because the definiens is predicated of the definiendum, and assent is not a predicate. And the minor premise is subject to denial. And the argument that the definiens only yields the quiddity or conceptualization by some aspect, the first by essential attributes and the second by accidental attributes, and both are predicates, is incomplete; for whoever permits acquiring conceptualization from assent would also permit obtaining the quiddity from something disparate. So ponder, and Allah knows the truth.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage addresses the epistemological question of whether conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) can be derived from each other, which would avoid circularity in theoretical knowledge. The author quotes the objection: all conceptualizations might be theoretical, but some assents are necessary, so one could acquire conceptualization from assent or vice versa, breaking the cycle. The response is a categorical denial: conceptualization cannot be known from assent nor assent from conceptualization. The reason for the first (no conceptualization from assent) is that a definiens (muʿarrif) must be a predicate of the definiendum (muʿarraf), but an assent (a proposition) is not a predicate. The author also mentions that the minor premise of that reasoning is deniable, and the argument that a definiens gives only quiddity (kuna) or conceptualization is insufficient because it would allow deriving quiddity from something entirely different. The passage ends with an encouragement to reflect.

Key Takeaway
The definiens must be a predicate, and since assent is a proposition (not a predicate), it cannot serve to acquire conceptualization.

Refutation of Causality Between Conception and Assent
Author refutes the claim that tasawwur causes tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the author's three objections to the claim that conception causes assent.

Core Idea
Causality between conception and assent fails because their existential domains differ and infinite regress results.

In this passage, the author challenges the widespread assumption that a conception (tasawwur) necessarily causes an assent (tasdiq). He offers three independent arguments—denial of context unity, infinite regress, and parity of aspects—that systematically dismantle the supposed causal link.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Find the claim being attacked: that tasawwur is a cause ('illa) of tasdiq. Understand the background: some logicians held that conception must precede and cause assent.
The passage begins with 'As for the first [objection]', implying a prior claim.
2
Extract the first objection
The author denies that cause and effect must share the same existential context (ẓarf). He gives examples: final causes are mental but their effects are external; mental forms are effects of God. Thus, context unity is not necessary.
He also refines the notion of 'kāsib' (acquisitor) as an accessory of a preparer (mu'idd), not a true cause.
3
Extract the second objection
If unity of context were required, an infinite regress would follow: a mental existent is contingent, so needs a cause; that cause must also be mental (due to unity), and so on ad infinitum.
The regress is vicious because it never terminates in a self-sufficient cause.
4
Extract the third objection
Assent (tasdiq) has two aspects: as knowledge (external) and as known (mental). Conception also has these aspects. Depending on which aspect is considered, both are either mental or both external, collapsing the asymmetry needed for a causal relation.
The author calls the opponent's argument a fallacy (mughālata).
5
Assess the overall critique
Evaluate whether the three objections individually or collectively undermine the causal claim. Consider the author's admission that he may not fully grasp the opponent's view.
The critique shows deep engagement with philosophical causality and its application to epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أولا : فلأن اتحاد الظرف بين العلة والمعلول ممنوع، لا سيما في العلل الناقصة، ألا ترى أن العلل الغائية موجودات ذهنية، ومَعَالِيلُها موجودات خارجية، والصور الذهنية معلولة للواجب ـ جل مجده .
وما قيل : إن العلة من لوازم المعلول، واللوازم منحصرة في المنطق في لوازم الماهية ولوازم الوجود، فأينما وجد المعلول وجد العلة ليس بشيء، لا لما قيل من أن الكواسب ليست عللا تامة ؛ فإن العلل وإن كانت ناقصة لازمة للمعلول، بل لأن الانحصار إنما هو للازم المعلول، ولزوم العلة ليس إلا بالنظر إلى الوجود الواقعي، أي كلما وجد المعلول في الواقع وجد العلة فيه، سواء كان في ذلك الظرف أو في غيره، وأيضا الكاسب ليس من العلل التي يلزم المعلول، بل من لواحق المعد الذي لا يجب اجتماعه مع المعلول، فلا لزوم أصلا .
وأما ثانيا فلأن وجوب اتحاد الظرف باطل، وإلا لزم التسلسل ؛ لأن الموجود الذهني ممكن، فلا بد له من علة ، والمفروض وجوب اتحاد الظرف ، فلها وجود في الذهن ، وهو أيضا ممكن ؛ لعدم حصول الواجب فيه، فلا بد له علة موجودة في الذهن ، وهكذا.
وأما ثالثا: فلأن للتصديق اعتبارین اعتبار قيامه بالذهن، واعتباره بلا شرط، وهو بالاعتبار الأول علم وموجود في الخارج على رأيه، وبالاعتبار الثاني موجود ذهني، ومعلوم وحكاية، وكذا حال التصور مرتبة العلم منه موجودة خارجية عنده، ومرتبة المعلوم موجودة ذهنية، فإن بني الكلام على أن الكاسب والمكتسب بالذات المعلوم فمعلوم التصور والتصديق كلاهما سيان في كونهما موجودين ذهنيين، وإن بني الكلام على أن الكاسب والمكتسب هو العلم فهما أيضا سواء في كونهما موجودين في الخارج، فظهر أن ما ذكره مغالطة، ولعل لكلامه وجها لستُ أُحَصِّله.
As for the first [objection]: The unity of context between cause and effect is denied, especially in the case of incomplete causes. Do you not see that final causes are mental existents, while their effects are external existents, and mental forms are effects of the Necessary Being—glory be to His majesty. And what is said: that the cause is among the necessary concomitants of the effect, and that necessary concomitants are confined in logic to concomitants of essence and concomitants of existence, so that wherever the effect is found, the cause is found—this is nothing, not because of what is said that the acquisitors (al-kawāsib) are not complete causes, for even if the causes are incomplete, they are necessary for the effect, but rather because the confinement is only for the necessary concomitant of the effect, and the necessitation of the cause is only with respect to real existence, i.e., whenever the effect exists in reality, the cause exists in it, whether in that context or in another. Also, the acquisitor (al-kāsib) is not among the causes that necessitate the effect, but rather among the accessories of the preparer (al-muʿidd) whose conjunction with the effect is not necessary, so there is no necessitation at all. As for the second [objection]: The necessity of unity of context is false, otherwise it would lead to an infinite regress; because the mental existent is contingent, so it must have a cause, and the assumption is that the context is unified, so it has an existence in the mind, which is also contingent because the Necessary Being does not subsist in it, so it must have a cause existing in the mind, and so on. As for the third: Because assent (taṣdīq) has two aspects: an aspect of its subsistence in the mind, and an aspect considered without condition. In the first aspect, it is knowledge and exists externally according to his view; in the second aspect, it is a mental existent, known, and a representation. Similarly, conception (taṣawwur) has a level of knowledge that is external in his view, and a level of the known that is mental. Therefore, if the discourse is built on that the acquirer and the acquired are essentially the known, then the known of conception and the known of assent are both equal in being mental existents. And if the discourse is built on that the acquirer and the acquired are knowledge, then they are also equal in being external existents. So it becomes clear that what he mentioned is a fallacy. Perhaps his statement has a face that I cannot grasp.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) in which the author refutes the claim that conception (taṣawwur) can serve as a cause (ʿilla) for assent (taṣdīq), a position held by some earlier logicians. The author presents three objections. First, he denies the necessary unity of context between cause and effect, citing examples such as final causes (al-ʿilal al-ghāʾiyya) which are mental while their effects are external, and mental forms which are effects of God. He also argues that the cause is not a necessary concomitant of the effect in the strict logical sense; rather, it is only concomitant with respect to real existence, but not necessarily in the same mental or existential context. The 'acquisitor' (al-kāsib) is not a true cause but an accessory of a preparer (al-muʿidd), which does not necessitate conjunction with the effect. Second, he argues that requiring unity of context leads to infinite regress, since every mental existent requires a cause, and if that cause must also be in the mind, the chain continues ad infinitum. Third, he analyzes the dual aspects of assent and conception: each can be considered either as knowledge (which is external) or as a mental representation. Depending on which aspect is taken, both conception and assent are either both mental or both external, so the claimed causal asymmetry collapses. The author concludes that the opponent's argument is a fallacy (mughālaṭa), though he acknowledges he may not fully grasp all nuances of that view.

Key Terms
تصور (taṣawwur): Conception; the mental representation of a thing without judgment (ḥukm). In this passage, it is considered in two respects: as knowledge (external) and as known (mental). [Note: In earlier logic (e.g., Avicenna), taṣawwur is simpler; here it gains complexity due to the critique of causal claims.]
تصديق (taṣdīq): Assent; a judgment (ḥukm) about a proposition. Here considered as having two aspects: subsistence in the mind and considered without condition. [Note: Standard definition; no significant shift.]
علة (ʿilla): Cause. The author distinguishes between complete and incomplete causes, and between cause and preparer (muʿidd). [Note: In logic, ʿilla often means middle term; here it is used in the broader philosophical sense of causality.]
ظرف (ẓarf): Context or 'container' (of existence); the domain in which a cause or effect exists. The author denies the necessity of unity of context. [Note: Standard technical term in discussions of causality.]
كاسب (kāsib): Acquisitor; the act or faculty that acquires knowledge. Here argued to be an accessory of a preparer (muʿidd), not a true cause. [Note: Term used by later logicians in the debate on whether conception causes assent.]
مُعِدّ (muʿidd): Preparer; a cause that predisposes but does not necessitate the effect. [Note: Standard in Islamic philosophy.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does requiring unity of context between cause and effect lead to an infinite regress?
A) Because the acquisitor is not a true cause.
B) Because mental existents are contingent and each requires a cause that must also be mental, leading to an endless chain.
C) Because final causes are mental while their effects are external.
D) Because conception and assent have dual aspects.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the causal link between conception and assent by arguing that context unity is not required, leads to infinite regress, and is rendered irrelevant by the dual aspects of both terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the author's three objections applies to the following claim: 'The conception of the eternal speech of Allah necessarily leads to assent to the Quran being uncreated.' Explain briefly.
?
Construct a counterargument using the author's first objection: 'Final causes are mental, but their effects are external. Therefore, context unity is not necessary for causality.' Apply this to a theological example.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Priority of Tasawwur and the Self-Refutation Objection
Defends priority of tasawwur with objection and solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why tasawwur is naturally prior to tasdiq and how the author resolves the self-refutation objection by distinguishing knowledge by essence and by accident.

Core Idea
The absolutely unknown cannot be judged, yet the judgment about it is valid by essence.

In this passage, the author argues that conception (tasawwur) must precede assent (tasdiq) because the absolutely unknown cannot be judged. An objection arises: the statement itself judges the unknown, leading to self-refutation. The author offers a nuanced solution distinguishing between knowledge by essence and knowledge by accident, preserving logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states that tasawwurat are presented first due to their natural priority (تعليهما طبعا). Find the reason given: 'for the absolutely unknown cannot be judged'.
2
Extract the objection
The objection says: if the unknown cannot be judged, then the proposition 'the absolutely unknown cannot be judged' itself is a judgment on the unknown, making it self-refuting (مبطل لنفسه).
This is a classic self-reference paradox.
3
Analyze the solution
The author answers by distinguishing two aspects: the unknown is 'known by essence' (معلوم بالذات) because its mental form exists, so judgment is valid in that respect; it is 'unknown by accident' (مجهول بالعرض) due to its unity with unknown external things.
Thus judgment and its negation can both be true from different perspectives.
4
Apply the distinction
Check how this distinction works in other Islamic examples, such as the 'illah in usul al-fiqh: the effective cause is known by its essence but unknown in its external applications.

التَّصَوُّرَاتُ الْقِسْمُ الْأَوَّلُ. التَّصَوُّرَاتُ قَدَّمْنَاهَا وَضْعًا لِتَقَدُّمِهَا طَبْعًا؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَجْهُولَ الْمُطْلَقَ يَمْتَنِعُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ. فَلَا بُدَّ لِلتَّصْدِيقِ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْمَوْضُوعِ وَالْمَحْمُولِ. قِيلَ: فِيهِ حُكْمٌ، فَيَكُونُ الْمَجْهُولُ مُبْطِلًا لِنَفْسِهِ، وَكُلُّ مُبْطِلٍ لِنَفْسِهِ كَذِبٌ، فَهُوَ كَذِبٌ. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّهُ مَعْلُومٌ بِالذَّاتِ لِحُصُولِ صُورَتِهِ فِي الْعَقْلِ، فَبِهَذَا الِاعْتِبَارِ يَصِحُّ الْحُكْمُ عَلَيْهِ، وَمَجْهُولٌ مُطْلَقٌ بِالْعَرَضِ لِاتِّحَادِهِ مَعَ الْأَشْيَاءِ الْمَجْهُولَةِ، فَالْحُكْمُ وَسَلْبُهُ بِالِاعْتِبَارَيْنِ. وَسَيَأْتِي فِي التَّبْصِرَةِ الَّتِي عُقِدَتْ لِتَحْقِيقِ الْمَحْصُورَاتِ. كَذَا فِي الْحَاشِيَةِ. وَتَفْصِيلُ الْمَقَامَيْنِ: أَنَّ هَهُنَا اعْتِرَاضَيْنِ: الْأَوَّلُ مَا ذَكَرُوا، وَالثَّانِي: أَنَّ انْعِقَادَ هَذِهِ الْقَضِيَّةِ مُشْكِلٌ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَحْصُورَةَ لَا بُدَّ فِيهَا مِنْ أَنْ يُلَاحِظَ عُنْوَانُ الْمَوْضُوعِ، وَيُجْعَلَ مِرْآةً لِمُلَاحَظَةِ الْأَفْرَادِ، وَلَا يَصِحُّ هَهُنَا؛ فَإِنَّهُ لَوْ جُعِلَ هَذَا الْمَفْهُومُ مِرْآةً لَصَارَتِ الْأَفْرَادُ مَعْلُومَةً، فَلَمْ يَبْقَ الْأَفْرَادُ أَفْرَادًا لِهَذَا الْمَفْهُومِ، وَيَكُونُ الْحُكْمُ عَلَى الْمَعْلُومَاتِ بِهَذَا الْحُكْمِ. وَجَوَابُهُ: أَنَّ الْقَدْرَ الضَّرُورِيَّ صِدْقُ الْعُنْوَانِ بِالْفِعْلِ أَوْ بِالْإِمْكَانِ، وَجَعْلُهُ...
Conceptions, the first section. We have presented them in order of arrangement because of their natural priority; for the absolutely unknown cannot be judged. Therefore, assent necessarily requires conception of the subject and predicate. It is said: in it there is a judgment, so the unknown would be self-refuting, and everything self-refuting is false, so it is false. The solution: it is known by essence because its form exists in the mind, so in this respect judgment upon it is valid, and it is absolutely unknown by accident due to its unity with unknown things, so judgment and its negation are according to two aspects. And it will come in the 'Tabsira' which is devoted to verifying quantified propositions. Thus in the gloss. The detailed explanation of the two positions: there are two objections here. The first is what they mentioned. The second is that the formation of this proposition is problematic; for a quantified proposition requires that the subject term be observed and made a mirror for observing individuals, and this is not valid here; because if this concept were made a mirror, the individuals would become known, so they would no longer be individuals of this concept, and the judgment would be upon known things with this judgment. Its answer: the necessary extent is that the term is true actually or potentially, and its making...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage from the beginning of the section on tasawwurat defends the natural priority of conception (tasawwur) over assent (tasdiq). The author argues that the absolutely unknown cannot be judged, so every judgment requires prior conception of the subject and predicate. An objection is raised: if the absolutely unknown cannot be judged, then the proposition 'the absolutely unknown cannot be judged' would itself involve a judgment on the absolutely unknown, thus refuting itself. The solution distinguishes two aspects: the absolutely unknown is known by essence (bi al-dhat) because its mental form is present, so judgment is valid in that respect; it is absolutely unknown only by accident (bi al-'arad) due to its connection with unknown external things. Therefore, judgment and its negation are true from different perspectives. A second objection concerns quantified propositions: the subject term must serve as a mirror for its individuals; but if the concept 'absolutely unknown' is used as a mirror, its individuals would become known, contradicting their being individuals of the unknown. The answer (partially given) is that it suffices for the term to be true of individuals actually or potentially, even if the individuals are not individually known.

التصديق
المجهول المطلق
معلوم بالذات
مجهول بالعرض

D) By rejecting the premise that the unknown cannot be judged

Key Takeaway
The priority of tasawwur over tasdiq is defended by showing that judgment of the unknown is possible through its mental form, using the distinction between essence and accident.

Quantified Propositions and the Unknown Subject
Answers second objection about quantified propositions with unknown subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for a quantified proposition (mirror of individuals) and explain the author's solution that truth can be actual or potential.

Core Idea
A quantified proposition requires the subject to be a mirror; the unknown still qualifies if the term is true actually or potentially.

A second, more technical objection concerns the formation of a quantified proposition like 'every absolutely unknown thing is not judged.' The subject term must serve as a mirror for individuals, but using it would make those individuals known, contradicting their unknown status. The author answers that it suffices for the term to be true of individuals actually or potentially, even if not individually known.

Guided Path
1
Understand the requirement of quantified propositions
In a quantified proposition (محصورة), the subject term must be a 'mirror' (مرآة) that allows the mind to observe all individuals under that concept.
This ensures the judgment applies to each individual.
2
Identify the problem
If the subject is 'the absolutely unknown,' making it a mirror would give mental presence to its individuals, making them known—so they would no longer be individuals of the absolutely unknown.
The objector argues that the proposition becomes impossible.
3
Analyze the author's answer
The author says the necessary requirement is that the term is true of its individuals either actually (بالفعل) or potentially (بالإمكان). Even if individuals are not known one by one, the term can still apply to them as a class.
This preserves the possibility of the quantified proposition.
4
Connect to the first distinction
Notice how this answer complements the earlier essence/accident distinction: the term is known by essence (its concept) and applies potentially to unknown individuals.

التَّصَوُّرَاتُ الْقِسْمُ الْأَوَّلُ. التَّصَوُّرَاتُ قَدَّمْنَاهَا وَضْعًا لِتَقَدُّمِهَا طَبْعًا؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَجْهُولَ الْمُطْلَقَ يَمْتَنِعُ عَلَيْهِ الْحُكْمُ. فَلَا بُدَّ لِلتَّصْدِيقِ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْمَوْضُوعِ وَالْمَحْمُولِ. قِيلَ: فِيهِ حُكْمٌ، فَيَكُونُ الْمَجْهُولُ مُبْطِلًا لِنَفْسِهِ، وَكُلُّ مُبْطِلٍ لِنَفْسِهِ كَذِبٌ، فَهُوَ كَذِبٌ. وَحَلُّهُ: أَنَّهُ مَعْلُومٌ بِالذَّاتِ لِحُصُولِ صُورَتِهِ فِي الْعَقْلِ، فَبِهَذَا الِاعْتِبَارِ يَصِحُّ الْحُكْمُ عَلَيْهِ، وَمَجْهُولٌ مُطْلَقٌ بِالْعَرَضِ لِاتِّحَادِهِ مَعَ الْأَشْيَاءِ الْمَجْهُولَةِ، فَالْحُكْمُ وَسَلْبُهُ بِالِاعْتِبَارَيْنِ. وَسَيَأْتِي فِي التَّبْصِرَةِ الَّتِي عُقِدَتْ لِتَحْقِيقِ الْمَحْصُورَاتِ. كَذَا فِي الْحَاشِيَةِ. وَتَفْصِيلُ الْمَقَامَيْنِ: أَنَّ هَهُنَا اعْتِرَاضَيْنِ: الْأَوَّلُ مَا ذَكَرُوا، وَالثَّانِي: أَنَّ انْعِقَادَ هَذِهِ الْقَضِيَّةِ مُشْكِلٌ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَحْصُورَةَ لَا بُدَّ فِيهَا مِنْ أَنْ يُلَاحِظَ عُنْوَانُ الْمَوْضُوعِ، وَيُجْعَلَ مِرْآةً لِمُلَاحَظَةِ الْأَفْرَادِ، وَلَا يَصِحُّ هَهُنَا؛ فَإِنَّهُ لَوْ جُعِلَ هَذَا الْمَفْهُومُ مِرْآةً لَصَارَتِ الْأَفْرَادُ مَعْلُومَةً، فَلَمْ يَبْقَ الْأَفْرَادُ أَفْرَادًا لِهَذَا الْمَفْهُومِ، وَيَكُونُ الْحُكْمُ عَلَى الْمَعْلُومَاتِ بِهَذَا الْحُكْمِ. وَجَوَابُهُ: أَنَّ الْقَدْرَ الضَّرُورِيَّ صِدْقُ الْعُنْوَانِ بِالْفِعْلِ أَوْ بِالْإِمْكَانِ، وَجَعْلُهُ...

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

10 min
Priority of Concepts and the Paradox of the Absolute Unknown
Commentary explains why concepts are prior and resolves the initial doubt about judging the absolute unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between essential and accidental knowledge to resolve the paradox that the absolute unknown cannot be judged.

Core Idea
The absolute unknown is known in itself by its mental form, making judgment possible; its unknowability is only accidental.

The commentator begins by asserting the natural priority of concepts over assents. A doubt arises: how can we judge the absolute unknown? The answer lies in a careful distinction between two ways of being known or unknown.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
The commentary states that concepts must precede assents because the absolute unknown cannot be judged. This sets up the problem.
2
Find the self-refutation argument
The passage says 'the unknown falsifies itself'—if the absolute unknown is truly unknown, then the statement 'the absolute unknown cannot be judged' is itself a judgment about it, leading to a contradiction.
3
Extract the resolution
The resolution is that the absolute unknown is known in itself (bi al-dhat) because its form occurs in the mind, yet it is unknown accidentally (bi al-'arad) due to its connection with external unknown things.
This dual consideration allows the judgment to be valid from the first perspective while maintaining the concept's extension.
4
Apply the resolution to the paradox
Judgment is valid as long as we consider the mental form; the unknownness only attaches accidentally. Thus the self-refutation is avoided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصورات
القسم الأول
التصورات
قدمناها وضعاً لتقدمها طبعاً؛ فإن المجهول المطلق يمتنع عليه الحكم. فلا بد للتصديق من تصور الموضوع والمحمول، (قيل فيه حكم)، فيكون المجهول مبطلاً لنفسه، وكل مبطل لنفسه كذب، فهو كذب، وحله: أنه معلوم بالذات لحصول صورته في العقل، فبهذا الاعتبار يصح الحكم عليه، ومجهول مطلق بالعرض لاتحاده بالأشياء المجهولة، فالحكم وسلبه بالاعتبارين. وسيأتي في التبصرة التي عقدت لتحقيق المحصورات، كذا في الحاشية. وتفصيل المقامين: أن ههنا اعتراضين الأول ما ذكروا، والثاني: أن انعقاد هذه القضية مشكل؛ فإن المحصورة لا بد فيها من أن يلاحظ عنوان الموضوع، ويجعل مرآة لملاحظة الأفراد، ولا يصح ههنا؛ فإنه لو جعل هذا المفهوم مرآة لصارت الأفراد معلومة، فلم يبق الأفراد أفراداً لهذا المفهوم، ويكون الحكم على المعلومات بهذا الحكم.
وجوابه أن القدر الضروري صدق العنوان بالفعل أو بالإمكان، وجعله مرآة لملاحظة الأفراد حال الحكم، وهذه القضية عرفية عامة، أو مشروطة عامة، وحاصلها: «كلما صدق عليه المجهول المطلق بالفعل في أحد الأزمنة أو بالإمكان فيمتنع عليه الحكم بالضرورة، بشرط كونه مجهولاً مطلقاً، أو دائماً ما دام مجهولاً مطلقاً بالعرض، وهذا الجواب يدفع إشكال المصنف أيضاً، ولا حاجة إلى التكلفات، لكن إن قرر الشبهة بقولنا: «كل مجهول مطلق دائماً يمتنع عليه الحكم دائماً فلا يكاد ينفع هذا الجواب ولا جواب المصنف.
نعم، قد يجاب بأنها قضية غير بينة وحاصلها: أن ما هو متصور معلوم لو صدق عليه مفهوم المجهول المطلق يمتنع الحكم عليه، فالحكم على المعلومات التي فرضت أنها مجهولة مطلقاً فهي معلومة بالذات، مجهولة مطلق بالعرض، فصدقها بالاعتبار الثاني، وصحة الحكم بالاعتبار الأول، وسيجيئ تقرير جواب المصنف في التبصرة.
ثم ههنا شك آخر، وهو أنا فرضنا أن شخصاً تصور مفهوم المجهول المطلق في مرتبة الهيولاني ابتداء، فالأشياء إما معلومة من وجه، وليس في العقل إلا مفهوم المجهول المطلق، أو مجهولة مطلقاً، فإن كانت معلومة وكانت معلومة بهذا المفهوم، ويكون هذا المفهوم صادقاً عليها، فيكون مجهولة مطلقاً، وقد فرض كونها معلومة فاستجمع المتنافيان، وإن كانت مجهولة مطلقاً فالمجهول المطلق صادق عليها، فوجه لها، وهو حاصل في الذهن، فصار الأشياء معلومة من وجه، لكنها مجهولة مطلقاً، هذا خلف.
وقد يجاب بأن الشيء إنما يصير معلوماً لحصول وجه من وجوهه، إن لم يكن ذلك الوجه منافياً للمعلومية؛ إذ ما يكون منافياً للمعلومية كيف يكون مبدأ
The concepts. The first section: The concepts. We placed them in order due to their priority by nature; for the absolute unknown cannot be judged upon. So assent necessarily requires a concept of the subject and predicate. (It is said: there is a judgment on it?) Thus the unknown falsifies itself, and everything that falsifies itself is false, so it is false. Its resolution: it is known by itself because its form occurs in the mind, so in this consideration judgment upon it is valid. And it is absolute unknown accidentally due to its unity with unknown things, so judgment and its negation are by two considerations. It will come in the Tabṣira which was made for the verification of enumerative propositions, as in the Ḥāshiya. And the detail of the two positions: there are two objections: the first is what they mentioned, and the second: that the composition of this proposition is problematic; for the enumerative proposition necessarily requires that the title of the subject be taken into account and made a mirror for considering the individuals, and that is not valid here; for if this concept is made a mirror then the individuals become known, so the individuals no longer remain individuals of this concept, and judgment would be upon known things with this judgment. Its answer: that the necessary extent is the truth of the title actually or potentially, and making it a mirror for considering the individuals at the time of judgment. And this proposition is conventional universal or conditional universal. Its summary: 'Whenever the absolute unknown is actually true of something at one time or potentially, then judgment upon it is necessarily impossible, provided it is absolute unknown, or perpetually as long as it is absolute unknown accidentally.' And this answer removes the problem of the author as well, and there is no need for forced constructions. But if the doubt is formulated by our statement: 'Every absolute unknown perpetually, judgment upon it is perpetually impossible,' then this answer and the author's answer are hardly beneficial. Yes, it may be answered that it is a non-evident proposition, and its summary: that what is conceived and known, if the concept of absolute unknown is true of it, judgment upon it is impossible. So judgment upon the known things that were assumed to be absolutely unknown: they are known by themselves, absolutely unknown accidentally. So their truth is by the second consideration, and the validity of judgment is by the first consideration. The exposition of the author's answer will come in the Tabṣira. Then here is another doubt: that we assumed that a person conceived the concept of absolute unknown in the hylomorphic stage initially. So things are either known in some respect, and there is nothing in the mind except the concept of absolute unknown, or absolutely unknown. If they are known and are known by this concept, then this concept is true of them, so they become absolutely unknown, while they were assumed known, so two contradictories combine. And if they are absolutely unknown, then the absolute unknown is true of them, and it has a face, and it is present in the mind, so things become known in some respect, but they are absolutely unknown; this is a contradiction. And it may be answered that a thing only becomes known by the acquisition of one of its faces, provided that face is not contrary to knowability; for how can that which is contrary to knowability be a principle...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Sullam al-Ulum dealing with the epistemological priority of concepts (tasawwur) over assents (tasdiq) and addressing the logical problem of how judgment is possible concerning the absolute unknown (al-majhul al-mutlaq). The commentator begins by affirming that we place concepts first because they are naturally prior; without a concept of the subject and predicate, no judgment can be formed. A doubt arises: if something is absolutely unknown, it cannot be judged, yet the statement 'the absolute unknown cannot be judged' itself seems to judge it. The resolution is that the unknown is known in itself (bi al-dhat) because its form is present in the mind—so in that respect judgment is valid—yet it is unknown accidentally (bi al-'arad) due to its connection with external unknown things. Thus judgment and its negation apply under different considerations. Two specific objections are then detailed. The first is the one just mentioned; the second concerns the nature of the universal proposition: an enumerative proposition (qadiyya muhsara) requires that the subject term be taken as a mirror for its individuals, but here the individuals of 'absolute unknown' would become known if so taken, negating the concept. The answer is that the proposition is either conventional-universal (urfiyya 'amma) or conditional-universal (mashruta 'amma): it applies only as long as the thing is absolutely unknown. This resolves the author's problem. Next, a further doubt is raised: if someone initially conceives the absolute unknown in the hylomorphic stage, then things are either known through that concept or absolutely unknown, leading to a contradiction. The answer begun is that a thing becomes known by acquiring one of its aspects, provided that aspect is not contrary to knowability. The commentary ends mid-sentence.

التصديق

بالذات

Key Takeaway
The absolute unknown is known in itself through its mental concept, allowing judgment, while its accidental unknownness does not prevent that judgment.

The Second Objection: The Universal Proposition and the Mirror Problem
Commentary resolves the second objection about the universal proposition and begins addressing a further doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the 'mirror' problem in enumerative propositions is resolved by classifying the proposition as conventional or conditional universal.

Core Idea
The universal proposition about the absolute unknown must be understood as conditional: it applies only as long as the thing is absolutely unknown.

The commentator addresses a second, more technical objection: if the absolute unknown is the subject of a universal proposition, then taking it as a mirror for its individuals would make those individuals known, contradicting the concept. The answer is that the proposition is not absolute but conditional, applying only when the individual is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Understand the mirror requirement
In an enumerative universal proposition, the subject term is used as a mirror to consider the individuals. For 'Every absolute unknown...' the individuals would become known, which is contradictory.
2
Identify the proposed solution
The answer is that the proposition is either conventional-universal (urfiyya 'amma) or conditional-universal (mashruta 'amma): it holds only when the thing is actually absolute unknown.
This means the proposition is not perpetual but conditional on the state of being unknown.
3
Consider the further doubt
A doubt is raised: if someone first conceives the absolute unknown in the hylomorphic stage, then things are either known or unknown, leading to a contradiction.
4
Examine the incomplete answer
The answer begun is that a thing becomes known by acquiring one of its aspects, provided that aspect is not contrary to knowability. The commentary ends here.
The last word 'مبدأ' suggests the answer was left incomplete.

التصورات
القسم الأول
التصورات
قدمناها وضعاً لتقدمها طبعاً؛ فإن المجهول المطلق يمتنع عليه الحكم. فلا بد للتصديق من تصور الموضوع والمحمول، (قيل فيه حكم)، فيكون المجهول مبطلاً لنفسه، وكل مبطل لنفسه كذب، فهو كذب، وحله: أنه معلوم بالذات لحصول صورته في العقل، فبهذا الاعتبار يصح الحكم عليه، ومجهول مطلق بالعرض لاتحاده بالأشياء المجهولة، فالحكم وسلبه بالاعتبارين. وسيأتي في التبصرة التي عقدت لتحقيق المحصورات، كذا في الحاشية. وتفصيل المقامين: أن ههنا اعتراضين الأول ما ذكروا، والثاني: أن انعقاد هذه القضية مشكل؛ فإن المحصورة لا بد فيها من أن يلاحظ عنوان الموضوع، ويجعل مرآة لملاحظة الأفراد، ولا يصح ههنا؛ فإنه لو جعل هذا المفهوم مرآة لصارت الأفراد معلومة، فلم يبق الأفراد أفراداً لهذا المفهوم، ويكون الحكم على المعلومات بهذا الحكم.
وجوابه أن القدر الضروري صدق العنوان بالفعل أو بالإمكان، وجعله مرآة لملاحظة الأفراد حال الحكم، وهذه القضية عرفية عامة، أو مشروطة عامة، وحاصلها: «كلما صدق عليه المجهول المطلق بالفعل في أحد الأزمنة أو بالإمكان فيمتنع عليه الحكم بالضرورة، بشرط كونه مجهولاً مطلقاً، أو دائماً ما دام مجهولاً مطلقاً بالعرض، وهذا الجواب يدفع إشكال المصنف أيضاً، ولا حاجة إلى التكلفات، لكن إن قرر الشبهة بقولنا: «كل مجهول مطلق دائماً يمتنع عليه الحكم دائماً فلا يكاد ينفع هذا الجواب ولا جواب المصنف.
نعم، قد يجاب بأنها قضية غير بينة وحاصلها: أن ما هو متصور معلوم لو صدق عليه مفهوم المجهول المطلق يمتنع الحكم عليه، فالحكم على المعلومات التي فرضت أنها مجهولة مطلقاً فهي معلومة بالذات، مجهولة مطلق بالعرض، فصدقها بالاعتبار الثاني، وصحة الحكم بالاعتبار الأول، وسيجيئ تقرير جواب المصنف في التبصرة.
ثم ههنا شك آخر، وهو أنا فرضنا أن شخصاً تصور مفهوم المجهول المطلق في مرتبة الهيولاني ابتداء، فالأشياء إما معلومة من وجه، وليس في العقل إلا مفهوم المجهول المطلق، أو مجهولة مطلقاً، فإن كانت معلومة وكانت معلومة بهذا المفهوم، ويكون هذا المفهوم صادقاً عليها، فيكون مجهولة مطلقاً، وقد فرض كونها معلومة فاستجمع المتنافيان، وإن كانت مجهولة مطلقاً فالمجهول المطلق صادق عليها، فوجه لها، وهو حاصل في الذهن، فصار الأشياء معلومة من وجه، لكنها مجهولة مطلقاً، هذا خلف.
وقد يجاب بأن الشيء إنما يصير معلوماً لحصول وجه من وجوهه، إن لم يكن ذلك الوجه منافياً للمعلومية؛ إذ ما يكون منافياً للمعلومية كيف يكون مبدأ
The concepts. The first section: The concepts. We placed them in order due to their priority by nature; for the absolute unknown cannot be judged upon. So assent necessarily requires a concept of the subject and predicate. (It is said: there is a judgment on it?) Thus the unknown falsifies itself, and everything that falsifies itself is false, so it is false. Its resolution: it is known by itself because its form occurs in the mind, so in this consideration judgment upon it is valid. And it is absolute unknown accidentally due to its unity with unknown things, so judgment and its negation are by two considerations. It will come in the Tabṣira which was made for the verification of enumerative propositions, as in the Ḥāshiya. And the detail of the two positions: there are two objections: the first is what they mentioned, and the second: that the composition of this proposition is problematic; for the enumerative proposition necessarily requires that the title of the subject be taken into account and made a mirror for considering the individuals, and that is not valid here; for if this concept is made a mirror then the individuals become known, so the individuals no longer remain individuals of this concept, and judgment would be upon known things with this judgment. Its answer: that the necessary extent is the truth of the title actually or potentially, and making it a mirror for considering the individuals at the time of judgment. And this proposition is conventional universal or conditional universal. Its summary: 'Whenever the absolute unknown is actually true of something at one time or potentially, then judgment upon it is necessarily impossible, provided it is absolute unknown, or perpetually as long as it is absolute unknown accidentally.' And this answer removes the problem of the author as well, and there is no need for forced constructions. But if the doubt is formulated by our statement: 'Every absolute unknown perpetually, judgment upon it is perpetually impossible,' then this answer and the author's answer are hardly beneficial. Yes, it may be answered that it is a non-evident proposition, and its summary: that what is conceived and known, if the concept of absolute unknown is true of it, judgment upon it is impossible. So judgment upon the known things that were assumed to be absolutely unknown: they are known by themselves, absolutely unknown accidentally. So their truth is by the second consideration, and the validity of judgment is by the first consideration. The exposition of the author's answer will come in the Tabṣira. Then here is another doubt: that we assumed that a person conceived the concept of absolute unknown in the hylomorphic stage initially. So things are either known in some respect, and there is nothing in the mind except the concept of absolute unknown, or absolutely unknown. If they are known and are known by this concept, then this concept is true of them, so they become absolutely unknown, while they were assumed known, so two contradictories combine. And if they are absolutely unknown, then the absolute unknown is true of them, and it has a face, and it is present in the mind, so things become known in some respect, but they are absolutely unknown; this is a contradiction. And it may be answered that a thing only becomes known by the acquisition of one of its faces, provided that face is not contrary to knowability; for how can that which is contrary to knowability be a principle...

The Paradox of the Absolutely Unknown
Author presents a paradox that the concept of the absolutely unknown would be known, and offers a rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the paradox that the concept of the absolutely unknown is self-defeating, and identify the two mutually exclusive concepts involved.

Core Idea
The concept of the absolutely unknown cannot be known without contradiction.

This lesson examines a classic dialectical puzzle in Islamic epistemology: if we claim to have a concept of 'the absolutely unknown,' that concept itself becomes known, creating a contradiction. We will walk through the paradox as presented in the source and understand why it forces a rethinking of how concepts are initially formed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
The author defines 'known' (ma'lum) as what is obtained by itself or through a mode, and 'absolutely unknown' (majhul mutlaq) as its opposite. Note that the two are mutually exclusive by definition.
Keep these definitions in mind; they are the key to the paradox.
2
Recognize the paradoxical claim
The author initially assumes that concepts (tasawwurat) are absolutely unknown, yet the concept of the absolutely unknown is a mode of those unknown concepts. But if this mode is in the intellect, it is itself known.
The mode itself is a concept, so it falls under the definition of 'known'.
3
Pinpoint the contradiction
The concept of the absolutely unknown is supposed to apply to the unknown, but as a known mode, it violates the mutual exclusivity of 'known' and 'absolutely unknown'. Thus, the two opposite concepts would have to coexist, which is impossible.
The contradiction forces us to reconsider whether the concept of the absolutely unknown can be initially obtained.
4
Articulate the paradox in your own words
Summarize: If we have a concept of the absolutely unknown, that concept is known. But it is a concept of what is unknown. Therefore, something known applies to something unknown, which is contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصورات للانكشاف، فنختار أنها مجهولة مطلقا، ومفهوم المجهول المطلق صادق عليها، ووجه من وجوهها، لكن لا يلزم من حصول هذا الوجه في العقل انكشاف الأشياء؛ لكونه منافيا للمعلومية، وهذا ليس بشيء؛ فإنا سمينا ما يكون حاصلا بنفسه، أو بوجه من وجوهه معلوما، ونقيضه مجهولا مطلقا، وإن شئت سمه بأسام أخر، ولا شك في تنافي المفهومين، ويلزم اجتماعهما بالبيان المذكور. وربما يجاب بمنع إمكان حصول مفهوم المجهول المطلق ابتداء، بل إنما يحصل ابتداء ما هو من أجلى البديهيات، كالشيية والوجود، فتأمل والله أعلم بالصواب.
Concepts: For disclosure, we choose that they are absolutely unknown, and the concept of the absolutely unknown is true of them, and it is a mode from among its modes, but it is not necessary from the occurrence of this mode in the intellect that things are disclosed, because it is contrary to being known, and this is nothing; for we have called what is obtained by itself or by a mode from its modes 'known', and its opposite 'absolutely unknown', and if you wish, name it other names, and there is no doubt about the mutual exclusivity of the two concepts, and their conjunction is necessitated by the aforementioned statement. And perhaps it is answered by preventing the possibility of the concept of the absolutely unknown being obtained initially, rather what is obtained initially is only from the most obvious of self-evident things, such as thingness and existence, so reflect, and Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about the possibility of having a concept of 'the absolutely unknown.' The author begins by noting that concepts are for disclosure, but then posits that they are absolutely unknown. He argues that the concept of the absolutely unknown applies to these concepts and is one of their modes. However, possessing this mode in the intellect does not necessarily disclose the things themselves, since it conflicts with them being known. He then defines 'known' as what is obtained by itself or by a mode, and 'absolutely unknown' as its opposite. The two concepts are mutually exclusive, yet the earlier reasoning forces them to combine. A possible answer is then offered: the concept of the absolutely unknown cannot be initially obtained; rather, initial concepts are only the most obvious self-evident things, like thingness and existence. This resolves the paradox by limiting which concepts can be formed first. The author concludes with 'tأمل' (reflect), indicating the depth of the issue.

Key Takeaway
The concept of the absolutely unknown is self-defeating because it would be a known mode of the unknown, forcing us to reconsider which concepts can be first formed.

Resolution: Initial Concepts Are Self-Evident
Author presents a paradox that the concept of the absolutely unknown would be known, and offers a rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the proposed resolution to the paradox: the concept of the absolutely unknown cannot be an initial concept; only self-evident concepts are initially obtained.

Core Idea
The paradox is resolved by denying that the concept of the absolutely unknown can be a first concept.

This lesson focuses on the rebuttal presented in the source: the concept of the absolutely unknown is not an initial concept. Instead, only the most obvious self-evident concepts, such as thingness (shay'iyya) and existence (wujud), are first obtained by the mind. This restriction avoids the contradiction while preserving the possibility of later forming the concept of the unknown through negation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rebuttal's core claim
The author suggests a possible answer: preventing the concept of the absolutely unknown from being obtained initially. This means the paradox does not arise at the first stage of concept formation.
The rebuttal is introduced with 'rubba yujabu' (perhaps it is answered), indicating it is a potential solution, not necessarily the author's final position.
2
Identify the initial concepts
According to the rebuttal, initial concepts are only the most obvious self-evident things (ajla al-badihiyyat), like thingness (shay'iyya) and existence (wujud). These are foundational and known directly.
These concepts are so basic that they cannot be unknown; they are the starting point of all cognition.
3
See how this resolves the paradox
If the concept of the absolutely unknown is not initially obtained, then the contradiction between 'known' and 'absolutely unknown' does not occur at the earliest level. Later, we can derive the concept of the unknown by negating the known.
This preserves the mutual exclusivity: the concept of the unknown as a derived concept does not claim to be a first concept.
4
Reflect on the deeper implication
The rebuttal implies that not all concepts can be formed at the outset; some are built from more primitive ones. This is a key insight in epistemology and logic.
Consider how this principle applies to other fields, such as fiqh, where foundational concepts like 'action' (fi'l) precede derivative concepts like 'obligatory' (wajib).
